The way to wealth is as plain as the way to market.
The majority of the wealthy men of Sydney, Melbourne
and Brisbane made their money by buying land in these
capital cities while it was low in price, and before these
cities were so large and populous as at present.
Western Australia
is the coming colony. Her Capital City will become
a second Melbourne or Sydney.
If you want to make money, buy land
there now. It is a good spec.

SPECIAL MEDICAL NOTICE.
Dr. G. H. RAYMOND,
Tub Eminent
Botanic Physician, Specialist L Medical Writer,
Has commenced Practice in Sydney, after a residence of
four years in Queensland, twelve months in Western Aus-
tralia, nearly three years in South Australia, and eighteen
months in the City of Melbourne.
The latter place Mr. Raymond was compelled to abandon
in consequence of the climate proving detrimental to his
health.
During DR. RAYMOND’S practical experience of nine
years in Australia, he has huilt up a reputation un-
approached by any other physician in chronic diseases in
the colonies. Da. Raymond has also relieved and cured
patients who had tried to no avail the so-called leading
medical talent in the world. Proof of this will be seen in
his valuable and scientific publications; and Dr. Raymond
would call the attention of deaf people to his latest scien-
tific paper on the “ Merits and Demerits of Artificial Ear-
Drums and Electro-Magnetic Treatment.”
DR. RAYMOND w ould also inform deaf persons that he
is the only AURIST in the British Empire that ever
exposed the fallacy of sticking ear-drums in the ear for
cure of deafness ; and, moreover, it is rank quackery for
any “Aurist” or “Oculist” to induce people to believe
that a mere external remedy will cure a disease, the root
of w'hich lies in the general constitution.
DR. RAYMOND is a Thorough Physician, as well as a
Specialist; hence his unrivalled success.
A series of Aural and Ophthalmic Papers, supplementary
to a volume published in 1887, will be sent on receipt of a
penny stamp. Address
No. 62 Carrington Street, Wynyard Square,
SYDNEY, N.S.W.
Works acknowledged by 11.R.H the Princess of Wales.
NOTE.—No mineral Poisons or the Knife used, nor
agonising blisters. Veritas odium par it!
Dr. Raymond intends shortly to Lecture on the eminent
American Medical Philosopher—Dr. Samuel Thomson,
founder of the Botanic System of Practice, and the first
man to expose the Errors and Fallacies of the Faculty.
Dr. Raymond will, when he mounts the public platform,
be the youngest medical lecturer and advocate in the
English-speaking world, he not having yet attained his
thirtieth year.

To H. F. Kloelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
t rmd into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consulted
in we l him one doctor, and during that time took a a rent
ijXianlitii of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, 1 became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. 1 agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At (he
end of three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time 1 commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. Abou t a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, 1 am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smyths, J.P.

DESCRIPTION.
The Platter or Bowl, marked
B, is the receptacle for holding
11b. of butter.
The Cover, marked A, is filled
with water at the top; as this
evaporates through the porous
cover, it produces a grea- degree
of cold, making the butter quite
HARD and EIRM during the
hottest Weather.
OUR INSTRUCTIONS EOB
, USE.
Soak the whole of the Butter
Cooler in clean water for twenty-
four hours. The butter can then
be placed in the dish marked B,
at the same time filling the hole
in the top with cold water.
The water must be replenished
from time to time as necessary,
but the whole cooler need only
be soaked once.

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 1, 1890.
Organisations of the Discontented.
I.— Communism.
Now that the inequity of social conditions
has caused a ferment in all civilised com-
munities, a brief enquiry into some of the
methods afoot for the amelioration of the
misery responsible for this discontent, can-
not be deemed superfluous, especially when
it is remembered that these organisations are
wholly misunderstood by the careless and
persistently misrepresented by the mone-
tarily-interested. In fact, to most people
the terms, Socialist, Communist, and
Anarchist, are synonyms for conspirator,
plunderer and assassin, and all who aim at a
re-construction of the social fabric are lumped
together as dynamiters and low-browed
desperadoes, associated for purposes of self-
aggrandisement, pledged to substitute for
existing institutions, a social order founded
on a complete disregard for all that is
universally regarded as honourable and
humane.
# # #
Communists or Humanists, those who
believe with Feuerbach that: The isolated
man is incomplete both as a moral and a
thinking being ; that: Isolation is finitude
and limitation, community is freedom and
infinitude, and from these statements draw
the conclusion that the Ego should be
merged in the Tu, the individual in the
community, that self-love should be replaced
by brotherly love, and that private property
should become collective—were first inter-
nationally united under that appellation at a
period memorable for European revolutions
—1847-8. At that time also was first pub-
lished their famous manifesto—the first de-
claration in favour of international amity
among wage-workers—the composition of
Karl Makx, the author of Das Kapital, and
Frederick Engels, his biographer, a work
which still broadly expresses their arguments
and aspirations.
# * *
The philosophical Communist asserts that
their movement is merely an evolutionary
continuation of a struggle that has been
waged since Time was, between freeman and
slave, patrician and plebeian, noble and
serf, guild-members and journeymen ; a con-
flict so bitter that it has rarely been sus-
pended except during revolutionary changes
or by the common ruin of the contentionists.
The latter-day victory of the middle-classes
over the patricians has by no means ended
the fight, merely narrowed the issue to a
tussle between the bourgeoisie and the
proletariat, a class hitherto only viewed as
pawns to bo used indiscriminately by either
side, and the Communist prophesies that
just as the upheaval of the bourgeoisie has
feudal and patriarchal rela-
tions, the privileges of rank and much
of the spoliation legalised under cover of
political and religious superstition*, so the
proletaire is inevitably destined to wring
from monopoly a tardy recognition of their
claims, ere eternally obliterating all class
distinctions. It is further argued that the
existence of widespread misery clearly
evinces an inequality of distribution and an
insufficiency of production, and as finality is
impossible in any department of human
activity in a world of ceaseless progression,
the Communist asserts that in thus prose-
cuting the search for human happiness, he is
merely fulfilling the primary object of life,
in that the chief end of man is the further-
ance of happiness for ail, at all times.
*
# #
Entirely disclaiming unity with rogues
and idlers, “the passive rot of the lowest
strata of society,” the Communist points out
that the domination of the bourgeoisie hav-
ing agglomerated population, centralised the
means of production, and assisted to concen-
trate property in the hands of the few, while
rendering labour-saving machines mere
instruments of bondage, surely proves that
this middle-class ascendancy has served its
purpose and is now a mere clog on the
development of the community. Though
previous social disagreements were caused,
as now, by class antagonisms, yet the sur-
roundings of the injured then made it
possible for them to maintain existence under
normal conditions, while to-day the condi-
tion of the worker relatively retrogrades so
rapidly that pauperism is developed more
speedily than either wealth or population.
This, to them, is plain proof that monopoly
cannot much longer hold its position, because
no longer capable of forcing its conditions
on society at large, and that the inability of
the modern monopolist to insure the existence
of his serfs even in a state of serfdom, his
inability and disinclination, at certain periods,
to support, instead of being sustained by the
proletariat, render his class-existence no longer
necessary or desirable. The bourgeoisie are
now maintained by the accumulation of the
savings of labour in private hands ; these
savings are the outcome of competitive
labour among the toilers excluded from
natural resources, but when production and
distribution is undertaken by universal
effort, the competition of individuals will be
hopeless, and the monopolists will be swal-
lowed up like rain-drops in the ocean.
• # #
Communists have no desire to form a
distinct party in opposition to other working-
men parties, their aims are ; The organisa-
tion of the Proletariat, the destruction of
middle-class supremacy and the conquest of
political power for the worker. From the
dawn of intelligence till now, say they, all
property-relations have been subjected to
constant change, so that the power and
privileges once monopolised by kings,
nobles, the Church, and afterwards by the
squirearchy, are now usurped by the
plutocracy. Thus, as the evolution of time
narrows the issues, the social problem yearly
becomes less difficult of solution. Com-
munists disavow any desire to confiscate
that form of property which is the product
of the worker’s unaided labour ; do not
propose to alter the ownership of that which
is wholly self-acquired ; they simply desire
the equitable dispersion of all that is now
centralised through the spoliation of the
Many by the Few. Values, they argue,
created under the prevailing wage-system
are usually exploited for the formation of
private capital, a species of property propa-
gated by the ceaseless withdrawal of fresh
blood from the very veins of the worker
by a phlebotomising capitalist who injuriously
exercises an important social function, for
allhough the utilisation of the capital
he controls is not dependent on his
possession or personal exertions and
can only be set in motion by communal
action, although the necessity which compels
others to use it reproductively affords him an
opportunity to divert the bulk of its returns,
yet lie has the power to withdraw it from
circulation at any moment. Under Com-
munistic conditions, the capital thus mono-
polised would be devoted to the promotion
of universal well-being and the increase of
the common store.
# # *
But, interjects someone, Communism will
destroy independence, individuality and
liberty. Hardly, replies the Communist,
merely bourgeoise independence, individu-
ality and license, the liberty of the slave-
holder to own the slave; in fact, all that
monopoly is most desirous of conserving.
You monopolists, say they, are wroth because
of our openly-expressed desire to destroy
private property ; but, to-day, the acqui-
sition of property, even in the smallest
quantities, is impossible to nine-tenths of
the community, and the fact that the bulk
of mankind are thus debarred from ob-
taining a portion of the world’s wealth
furnishes you with your opportunity.
What Communism would fain accomp-
lish is not the mere destruction of private
property-holding, but the annihilation of the
monopoly of opportunities. When labour
can no longer be transformed into rent,
interest, or profit, says the bourgeoisie, then
ambition and individuality will bo extin-
guished. It is plain, interjects the Com-
munist, by your iteration of the term
individuality that nothing more is at stake
than the individuality of the property-
holder ; accepting for the nonce your limited
application of the term, we undoubtedly
desire to abolish such an individuality, for it
is the monster that has swallowed up the
individuality, liberty, and happiness of the
bulk of the human family.
The monopolist also argues that with the
abolition of bourgeoisie property all incen-
tives to labour would cease, and universal
idleness prevail. On the other side it is
asserted that were this possible social chaos
would long ere this have arrived, for, under
the prevailing system of competitive pro-
duction for profit and monopolistic distri-
bution, those who toil not are ever adding to
their store, while those who toil for all
obtain no possessions worthy the name, and
therefore, from the monopolists’ standpoint,
are without incentives to labour. Of course,
says the Communist, it is perfectly natural
that the property-holder should imagine that
the present systems of production and dis-
tribution are eternal verities instead of mere
passing relations in social and economic
evolution, nor is it difficult to understand
why the joyless machines whose exertions
maintain him in his position, should, in so
many cases, be unaware that they have any
good reason to consider the matter at all, or
that their consideration could, in any way,
tend to its alteration.
# # *
The Communist, furthermore, claims that
the attainment of supremacy by the proletary
will bring about universality of industrial
production, and erase national and racial
antagonisms, for when the spoliation of one
individual by another ceases, then the
spoliation of weak nations by the strong is
doomed. Thus the barbarous and unreason-
ing hostility that demands expression in the
establishment of standing armies and navies
will be eradicated and the interchange of
products and ideas facilitated. This supre-
macy will also be signalised by the with-
drawal of capital from the present con-
tracted channels, the centralisation of all
productive forces in the hands of the users
and an intelligent endeavour to use all natural
resources to the best advantage.
# * *
At the outset this programme involves an
apparently despotic interference with what
many term the “rights of property,” though
how the inanimate can have any “rights ” is
not very clear, but if, for the sake of argu-
ment, it should be granted that property has
rights, as its office is the maintenance of life,
then it is surely evident that such rights
must be held in abeyance whenever they
begin to clash with those of animate human
beings. The interference, however, is
unavoidable as a primary means towards a
necessary change, and the Communist de-
clares that it will be justified by results.
For when class distinctions have disappeared,
class antagonisms will be forgotten ; when
political power is chieffy centred in the hands
of the industrious, then the power of the few
to oppress the many will be nullified and the
class-supremacy of the proletary having per-
formed its office, will die out like the other
dominations that have preceded it and be
merged in a universality of effort and inter-
est. In these latter days everyone’s needs
will be satisfied from the common stock ; no
one will possess aught that he cannot per-
sonally put to good use, and no arena will
exist for the monopoly of opportunities that
compels the sturdy and intelligent non-pro-
perty-owner to beg leave tj toil from the
effortless monopolist. Then will land, pro-
ductive instruments, and means of transit
become commonwealth, and justly, since
from their nature they can only be
thoroughly employed by general combina-
tion. Then will the extremes of wealth and
penury, titular and class distinctions,
racial animosities, sexual disabilities, religious
feuds, idleness, ignorance and crime be
minimised or annihilated. Then will there
be no limit to the free development of every
virtuous faculty ; all will be busy, well-
informed, well-treated, free and therefore
moral, because “the free development of
each is the free development of all.” Never-
theless, the millennium is, happily, unattain-
able. Happily unattainable, simply be-
cause the ideal can never be realised;
because anticipation is more enjoyable
than realisation ; because each barrier to
human progress when overthrown, becomes
a watch-tower from whence fresh obstacles
may bo surveyed ; because the loftier the
eminence the wider the horizon, and with a
more varefied mental atmosphere hitherto
unsuspected evils will be revealed, fresh
avenues for thought and effort disclosed,
and still higher eminences unveiled.
a * *
To recapitulate: Communism may be
briefly described as the organisation of
society into industrial groups, whereof each
member labours according to ability for the
common weal, while the personal return is
more nearly apportioned to wants than with
regard to results. Here let their motto
speak : From each according to capacity, to
each according to necessity. Of course the
individualist asserts that this supplying of
wants from commonwealth, unless the return
be made proportionate to the recipient’s
actual contributions, Avould remove the chief
incentive to labour and discourage the in-
dustrious. The disciples of Blanc and
Cabet reply that this argument exactly
describes the present situation, that now
those who work hardest earn least and
that the privileged idlers everywhere receive
the bulk of the earnings of toil. Also that
in no community is production invariably
approximate to effort. This is particularly
true of the blundering worker, whose exer-
tions may be equal to or even greater than
those of the expert, whose skill enables him
to perform with ease what the other executes
with difficulty. Therefore, the former’s
waste uf tissue is probably as large, it not
larger than cnat of the latter ; consequently
his necessities are, at least, equally great
and so it is unjust that he should receive a
lesser portion of the sustenance required for
the repair of that waste, especially when it is
remembered that at present even skilled
workers receive little more than what is
absolutely requisite to the support of fif e>
Furthermore, that all men do not labour
solely for pay, that they are compelled, in
many cases to do their utmost by a high sense
of duty and the demand of the faculties for
active expression and developmen t. In support
of this statement, they point to the uure-
munerated efforts of bygone poets, philoso-
pliers, patriots, prophets, scientists, explorers
inventors and leaders of advancement
throughout the world, and declare that the
evolution of society will elevate mankind to
an altitude where Communism is the only
system compatible with the prevalent ideas
of justice, to a state wherein all are eager
to perform their share of the daily toil, and
the chief aim of each dweller will be the
furtherance of the general good by the devo-
tion of all his energies to the service of the
community. Then ; “ Law will have become
unnecessary, Communism will be the natural
expression of social life, and perfect liberty
will be the lot of all, because each shall have
become a law unto himself.”
* * *
On this distant goal the eyes of the Com-
munist are ever bent, while round his brows
are bound this appeal : “ The workers have
nothing to lose but their chains ; they have
a world to win. Proletarians of all nations
unite.” His effort to further the free expan-
sion of human aspirations cannot fail to be
beneficial in many ways, even if it do not
fully achieve its purpose, for in order to
arrive at any height of excellence it is neces-
sary that the ideal should be a loftier one
than can reasonably be deemed possible of
accomplishment.
The Gathering of the Colonels.
The population of Australia consists of some
three million human beings—and they promise
before long to be mostly colonels* In the
obtrusively “loyal” colony of Victoria, in
fact, they are mostly colonels already, and tie
man who is not a colonel is generally either a
captain or a lieutenant, and tiie person who is
neither a colonel, a captain nor a lieutenant has
grievously misused the opportunities bestowed
upon him by Providence, or else he is a cripple
and is thus debarred from displaying himself in
the gorgeous panoply of war. In the Victorian.
Parliament, “the Party of the Colonels” has
become a recognised political institution, which
increases in strength at each succeeding elec-
tion, and in course of time it will probably
develope into a “ War Party,” and become
actively aggressive, whereas, so far, it has only
been passively obnoxious. It is much given to
wearing uniform in season and out of season,
but principally out of season; it is badly
addicted to strategy and fortifications and
complex problems relating to guns; and it
professes to regard Australia as a great military
State, and to consider every political question
which' comes before it exclusively from the
standpoint of the “army.” The component
parts of tills rampant faction consist chiefly of
the tameless softgoodsman, and the unquench-
able person in the wholesale tea interest, and the
bootmaking hero, and other small, fat, brown-
EHannibals of moreor less importance; but
.se they are privileged to blunder about in
front of an awkward squad of militia they wear
their titles at all hours of the day and night,
and the melancholy page of Han sard is mostly
devoted to recording the observations of the
Major-General on strategical railways, and the
outpourings of the Colonel on torpedoes, to-
gether with the remarks of sundry lieutenants
and a large quantity of loose captains on things
at large. And, considering the number and
ferocity of this military oligarchy, it becomes a
serious question whether the country might not
do well to crowd through with a smaller supply
of colonels. An army of some kind is, unfortu-
nately, a necessary evil, but wdieu an army is
nearly all colonels, and almost every colonel is in
Parliament, and they all feel called upon to run
the affairs of the nation on a war-footing, and
to look down with contempt upon the common
herd which doesn’t make an exhibition of itself
with a banner and a gun, the country might
find it safer to abolish its alleged troops and
chance the consequences.
* # #
The political party which is made up of the
Victorian colonels and their supporters is dis-
tinguished chiefly for a certain military Toryism
which is even a shade more offensive than the
worst specimen of ordinary unarmed Toryism
known in this country, and should a similar
party form itself in the other Australian legis-
latures, it will almost inevitably be distin-
guished by the same objectionable characteris-
tics. The local colonel is a howling Conservative
by reason of his clothes. He is permitted to
shine at Government House in the glaring
colours of a bird of paradise, and as the sergeant
of police—who also wears a uniform—is ex-
cluded, and flic clerk in the Lands Department
who, like the colonel, is a public servant, but
a servant without a sword or a decoration —is
seldom admitted and is of no account when he
is admitted, therefore the colonel regards him-
self as the special prop of vice-royalty, and as a
being apart from the nation at large. In his
military capacity lie takes his orders fi in the
Government, but as the Government takes its
orders from Parliament, and the Victorian Par-
liament is largely composed of an accumulation
of colonels, he has a dual capacity, and his
oppressive dignity is rendered almost incurable
in consequence. Why the colonel should be
allowed to inflict himself on the Legislature of
a peaceful country, when the other public ser-
vant who dusts the street with a broom and a
shovel is entirely unrepresented, has never been
explained. Except in military matters he is
almost useless as a legislator, and Australia has
no military problems on hand. He iS haunted


w ith * craving for strategical railways to
be constructed regardless of cost to all man-
ner of desolate spots on the lovely sea-shore,
s 0 that when the hazy and indefinite thing
known as “ the enemy” presents itself the local
f or ces may be promptly conveyed by rail to a
nlace where they will be of no use to anybody,
and from which they will probably be unable
t 0 g e t back. He is always anxious to raise
more troops and to create more colonels, and
then the new regiments fade away mysteriously,
and only the colonels are left. He never loses a
chance to demand that some more expensive
fortifications shall be put up in a hollow place
jn the middle of a dense forest where there are
no roads and no inhabitants—in short he suffers
f ro m a mistaken impression that Australia is a
military state run by an oligarchy of colonels,
an d unless something is done to convince him of
his mistake trouble of some kind will probably
ensue.
« * *
The scheme of building up a “great Australian
army” to maintain the interests of Imperialism
and grovel, regarding which Sir Henry Parkes
has Avasted so much enthusiasm, and thereby
ruling these lands on a semi-military system
under the guidance of some idiotic Cromwell
or small, fat-headed Caosar, has already gone too
far. The softgoods colonel may be mean and
insignificant, but the autocrat with a cotton
umbrella is as objectionable as the auto-
crat Avith a sceptre, and a despotism of
armed importers and ironmongers and lawyers
would be probably a shade more oppressive and
certainly more ignominious than the despotism
of a Moltke. The man avlio dies, however
uselessly, at the cannon’s mouth may preserve a
certain dignity even in his fall, but the person
who is shot with a dead cat on a raft is not
tragic —he is only ridiculous. The fact that the
Australian colonel is short and fat and wheezy
and only a badly-disguised draper at the best,
and that he is miserably ignorant of the rudi-
ments of his alleged profession in addition to
his other sins, is merely a reason why he should
be the more promptly suppressed; these
features in his character may render him useless
for purposes of national defence, but they make
him none the less dangerous to the country
which he infests. The officer who has neither
brains nor intelligence, and who hangs on by
clutching his saddle with one hand and his
horse’s mane with the other, would only arouse
the derision of an invader, but he is as capable
as any other man of rushing a crowd of peaceful
citizens and demonstrating his zeal by shooting
at an unarmed population which is unable to
shoot in reply. The two prominent features of
the genuine or military colonel are that he is a
soldier in the first place and a grovelling Tory
in the second ; the softgoods colonel is a
decided failure as a soldier, but he fulfils the
second part of his duty to perfection —as a Tory
he has no equal anywhere on earth.
* * *
The case of this rising oligarch admits of only
two possible remedies. The first and the most
obvious is to abolish him in his capacity as a
politician and by keeping him for use instead
of merely for show to Avipe out the military
aristocracy which is gradually coming into
existence in this peaceful land. Nations which
li\'e by war may, with some show of reason,
convert the inflated colonel and major-general
into a sort of nobility, but as Australia has no
present desire to conquer any adjacent country
and has no intention which up to date has been
confided to The Bulletin of starting on a path
of gore and slaughter, the Australian officer is
only an ordinary public servant and should be
treated as such. In actual A'alue lie is much
below the country policeman with the pro-
minent feet, and in usefulness the individual
who shunts trucks on a railway is greatly his
superior. His dignity is based entirely on the
fact that a peaceful nation has chosen to make
itself as far as possible a slavish imitation of
the harassed and oppressed communities of the
Old World ; because the despotic soA'ereigns of
Europe are surrounded by military hirelings as
the only possible safeguard against assassination,
therefore Australia —in default of the approved
type of active butcher—has raised the tailor to
military dignity, and lent him a certain fictitious
importance by allowing him to fool round as
the armed guardian of vice-royalty. As
nobody wants to shoot an Australian Governor,
and as the soft-goods officer would probably be
too scared to interfere supposing any one made
the attempt, that warrior is of exactly the same
\'alue as a navy to an inland state, or a priest
to a nation which recognises no god. There-
fore the city colonel, who exists solely on the
strength of his clothes and his dignity, should
be restricted to his purely military duties, if
he has any ; he should be disqualified for a seat
iu the Legislature where his anxiety to prove
that lie is a soldier instead of a disguised trader
makes him clamour incessantly for more guns
and torpedoes and fortifications ; and he should
he restrained someAvhat in the use of his title
seeing that the nation has no use for a colonel
while ho is engaged in supervising the accounts
°f a building-society, and only wants him while
actually on duty. Also there should be a good
deal less of him. It is difficult to throw a brick
m any given direction without injuring a colonel,
and this superfluity is strangely out of place in
a country which has no warlike traditions, and
no desire to go out and kill anybody except
nuder the influence of actual necessity.
« # #
I’he other alternative is to abolish the whole
crowd of overAvhelming military dignitaries in
one act, but possibly this comprehensive mea-
sure might be premature and inadvisable. The
Australian colonel is a. costly nuisance; the
great object of his existence is to get up a
general conscription, and put everybody in
uniform, and run the entire country on a mill-
; ar y basis, with a howitzer and a state of siege
and an entrenched camp and other warlike
appurtenances, and no reasonable being expects
"bat he would answer any conspicuously useful
Purpose in case of war ; but it is just possible
nvt his presence might scare a weak-minded
and uneducated enemy for a day or two till
someone else had time to arrange a plan of
defence. Therefore, the country may resolve
0 tolerate him in a modified or alleviated form,
J 'least fora time. But if the warlike account-
an - and the blood< hirsty conqueror in the *©a-
trade, and the rampant mining - speculator,
whose feet are so big that every _ horse
he mounts considers itself in shafts,
persist in their attempt to manage the
world on military principles, their abolition
will become inevitable. The formation of a
military party in the Legislature is miserably
out of place in a land which has no enemies and
no desire to acquire any, and the formation of
a military spirit in the country is merely the
assiduous cultivation of a desire to kill some-
body—a process which is apt to end disastrously.
If Australia is ever invaded, it will probably
be because one of her cheap, second-hand
Napoleons has repeated the stupendous blunder
of the Soudan, and has recklessly hit an oppo-
nent who is large enough to retaliate. Ireland
came to grief through an insane attempt to run
eight or ten kings simultaneously some centuries
ago, and the ceaseless accumulation of colonels
in these colonies promises, sooner or later, to
be almost equally dangerous.
Selfishness.
Despite the overcrowded market of ethical
literature it seems nevertheless impossible to find
a writer who honestly deals with social ques-
tions arguing thereon with due regard to first
principles. As an instance of this, take the
case of Selfishness and Disinterestedness, the
former being assumed as a vice, and the latter
as a virtue. Can there be any greater false
premise in logic than this? One frequently
hears the phrase repeated that So-and-so is a
selfish person, and, consequently, objectionable ;
therefore, to those who imagine that they have
ever performed in their lives what they suppose
to be a disinterested action, a few remarks may
profitably be addressed. It is evident that
man is still under very erroneous impressions
regarding his ideas of human motives. Meta-
physics clearly and unquestionably prove that
selfishness is the mainspring of every slightest
action in life. No human being ever acted
without a motive, else he would not act at all,
and no motive under the sun can survive
microscopic investigation. Either directly or
indirectly every deed we perform is for our own
benefit in some way or another, and it is only
through a long existing aberration of intellect
that rational organisations believe and persuade
themselves that they are generous, disinterested
beings.
# * *
It is marvellous how readily a kind action
done reconciles a man to himself. Conscience
whispers “ What a good fellow you arc !” and
straight away the benefactor hurries to the bar
and shouts for himself, exulting in his self-
conscious goodness. There is also another
motive influencing men to benevolent action,
which, although the one most generally lauded,
is nevertheless the most interested of the lot,
namely, “ doing good for the sake of everlast-
ing happiness,” a recommendation of a certain
antediluvian fossil by name Paley. There is
another feature of subtle self-flattery in the
condescension of the well-to-do lounger en-
dowed with all the favours of fortune, associat-
ing in an assumed equality with some unfor-
tunate to whom fate has assigned the darker
paths of circumstance. In this case it is the
conscious sense of power which gratifies his
vanity in the exercise of the protective faculty,
and, as regards the oft-quoted beautiful disin-
terestedness which is a feature in the frank
readiness wherewith youth contracts friend-
ships, no greater rubbish has yet offended
thoughtful minds. It is with no spirit of cal-
culation that such contracts are formed, yon
will say ; but this is a mistake, as youth is
drawn into companionship half by some void
within itself and half by some quality which
appears to supply that void. Some do the
very finest things out of an exalted self-esteem,
carrying their point of honour so high that
there is no sacrifice they are not capable of
making provided it only serve to elevate their
opinion of themselves. However, the investi-
gators of motives can always prove to the un-
prejudiced inquirer that the philanthropist acts
for love of fame; the woman of self-sacrifices
and good works is consciously posing before an
admiring world, or that some other equally per-
sonal considerations impel action, moral or im-
moral, indiscriminately.
When the starving mother takes the last
morsel of bread from the locker and hands it
over to her infant at the cost of her life, she
does so because by that action she derives more
pleasure than she would were she to eat it her-
self. This cannot fairly be held to morally
detract from the beauty of her action, it merely
serves as an illustration of the difference between
philanthropy and anti-socialism. Virtue exists
in man not in the concentration of his
happiness in himself, but in the seeking thereof
in others : but in either case his deeds will be
selfish and interested, and it is therefore evi-
dently desirable that the correct definition of
selfishness be recorded in the human brain for
evermore. For the . present the best word
corresponding to the idea sought to be con-
veyed is egoism— i. e. , a vice, and for the con-
verse socialism— i.e. , a virtue. Selfishness and
disinterestedness have no logical affinity with
their pristine significations.

West Sydney Election.

than all, a victory at the ballot-box on Saturday
would have., been an example and a direction to
Labour bodies everywhere, and have endowed
the ensuing general elections with a new' and
specific character. Unfortunately, Labour
blundered away all these advantages last Satur-
day. Mr. A. G. Taylor possesses his seat by
the votes of Labour, but his success derogates
from the authority and influence of the
Labour council. Instead of being the pro-
duct of the council’s energies his candidature
w r as a contumelious rejection of the advice and
action of the entire Labour body. No matter
what Mr. Taylor’s abilities may be, no matter
how democratic his sentiments or radical his
conduct, no matter how suitable a mouthpiece
he might make for the masses in Parliament,
the fact that the Labour organisation had
selected another man for the Labour vote
should have made him, if a true friend of the
cause, stand aside on an occasion when a test
case was practically submitted to the inspection
of the public. And it did not matter if the
nominee of the Council w ere the reverse of all
Mr. Taylor claims to be. The particular man
was not the primary consideration ;it was the
demonstration by Labour of the fact that it
possessed the knowledge to select and the power
to return to Parliament a man of its own. That
demonstration stands now indefinitely post-
poned. More than that, a precedent has been
set which will heavily w eight future decisions
of the Council respecting Parliamentary elec-
tions. What Mr. Taylor did successfully
others similarly wdlful in temper and character
will try to do, and whether they win or lose
they will bo thorns in the flesh of organised
Labour. Now' that the evil is so unmistakably
disclosed it should be grappled with at once,
and, if needs be, knifed from the system. The
man who sets himself up to defeat Labour’s
nominee should receive no quarter from the
hands of Labour. A few more blunders like
that perpetrated by the Labour party at the
West Sydney election will prove the leaders
hopelessly incompetent to exercise any influence
in the Parliamentary world.
A Permanent Agreement.
Ever since the first beginning of the revolt of
humanity it has been the favourite complaint of
the Capitalist that there is no end to the
demands of Labour. The concession of yester-
day has been followed by the demand for fresh
concessions to-day, and the concession of to-day
lias only paved the way for new' encroachments
to-morrow, while beyond this point lie all the
uncounted days of time, to be spent by the
workman in making further inroads on the
privileges of the rich. Consequently, the organs
of Capital are loud in their demands for a “final
settlement.” Hitherto all the conventions
entered into have only been of temporary effect,
but now' the time has come to draw a definite
line, and declare that concession shall never go
any further, and the Federated Shipowners of
Great Britain have resolved, should all other
means fail, to organise the must stupendous
boycott know'll in history, by way of bringing
about “a reasonable and permanent agreement”
which will last till the earth and the labour
problem is worn out: —
The Times to-day publishes a statement that the mem-
bers of the Shipowners’ Federation, being determined to
submit no longer to the continual aggressiveness of the
unions, have decided to lay up ali vessels in every port in
the United Kingdom unless the unionists agree to con-
clude a reasonable and peimanent agreement.
The Times heartily applauds this decision of the Ship-
owners’ Federation as the only means of checking the
intolerable evils of the present system, which keeps the
shipping trade in a constant ferment owing to the never
ending disputes.
The one point which the associated capitalists
seem to have overlooked in this programme is
that nothing on this earth is permanent, and
consequently they are attempting to- do some-
thing which all the world has tried in vain since
the beginning of history. There never will be
any end to the demands of Labour, and to
attempt to bring them to an end is an attempt
to tie-up the ocean with a string. Neither will
there ever be an end to the demands of Capital
so long as Capital lasts—so the situation is
exactly equal. To bring about the “ permanent
agreement ” concerning which the employers
have wasted so much eloquence, it is necessary
in the first place that the employer should be
immortal, and in the second that the employe
should live for ever ; but on the very day when"
that convention is signed the men who signed it
will commence to die off and new men will fill
their places, and as the dead cannot control the
living, the “permanent agreement” begins to
fall to pieces before the ink is dry. And in en
this is only the first stage of the difficulty. If
the scheme of the Shipowners’ Federation is
ever to be carried out, that body must be in a
condition to guarantee that the price of food
will never go up, and that the value of money
will never go down, and that rent, clothing,
taxation, and a hundred similar things will
undergo no more fluctuations. They must
undertake that there shall be no further
changes in shipbuilding, that steam will never
be superseded by any other power, that no
new inventions in machinery will disturb the
present conditions of labour, that the
trade of Britain will never leave her, that
her ports w ill not silt up, and that the sea itself
will not lie one day abandoned as a highway,
and aerial navigation render ships useless and
superfluous. They have got to harness down
the earth, the air, and the ocean, and. to make
sure that no convulsion of nature will interfere
with the course of trade, and they must chain
up the elements of war, revolution, and civil
commotion, and lodge a. caveat against the
development or retrogression of humanity, and
serve an injunction upon Providence lest any
influence which is not covered by the precau-
tions already enumerated shoukl interpose, and
set the agreement at defiance. And even when
all these things are done Labour and Capital
will both go on making fresh demands so long
as they both survive. There is no finality in
anything. Probably the old Carthaginian ship-
owner, . who put to sea in a top-heavy galley
with a half-ton of stone in place of an anchor
and the figure of Baal or Melkarth at the
bow, w r as confronted by the same difficulty,
and if so the chances are that he also fixed up a
“permanent agreement,” and settled the labour
problem for ali timo. Yet if be could rise from
his grave to-day he would find that his agree-
ment and a good many others of an equally per-
manent kind that succeeded it had been so
utterly forgotten that no one knew they had
ever existed.
Cash and Dignity.
Mb. Alexander Brown’s notice of motion, in
the New South Wales Assembly, for an all-
round increase in Ministerial salaries is a
feather-brained proposal which neither Parlia-
ment nor the community are at present likely
to seriously entertain, and might accordingly be
silently passed over were not it that Mr. Brown
states reasons for his motion. One of those
reasons is that the salaries of the present day
are “ not compatible with the maintenance of
the dignity ” of those who fill Parliamentary
offices. If there were no other reason for reject-
ing the motion this one would amply justify
opposition. It weds honour and money and, if
accepted, would extend still further the baleful
influence of the , large bank-balance. Some
money is certainly a necessity, and every
worker should receive fair pay for the work he
does ; but if there be dignity anywhere about,
it is in the work, and not in the pay, that it
resides. The Minister who cannot preserve his
dignity at current rates has altogether too
slippery a possession. What Mr. Brown means
by “ dignity ” is, possibly, ability to entertain
persons in lavish style and go about in social
splendour, but if so, ‘ ‘ dignity ” is the wrong
term for the exhibition. And the people have
got nothing to do with the entertainment of
Ministers or the appointments of their houses.
A Minister is paid for doing work, and whether
he gyrates in knee-breeches in the drawing-
rooms of Fashion, or
Goes to his garret, where he nightly rocks
His wizened infant in its candle-box
when his work is done, is of no concern to the
public. Whatever way his taste inclines he can
go, but he is not to make his taste a cause for
larger remuneration for his work. There are
several members in the present New South
Wales Parliament who hold Mr. Brown’s
opinions on Ministerial salaries, though they do
not adopt his language. These say bluntly that
the work of a Minister is worth more than £ISOO
a year. Even Mr. Francis Abigail, to whom
£ISOO a year passed for a while, declares that it
never paid him for the ability and industry
be expended at his post. These advocates for
an increase in salaries can be met squarely Avhew
they put their claim properly forward. Aus-
tralia is not unwilling to pay her statesmen
adequately, and no question of an exhausted
exchequer or depressed financial conditions
should stave off the demand for fair remunera-
tion, if at present it is unfair. But it may be
pointed out that if Mr. Brown’s scale be
adopted the Parliamentary officers of the
people, as apart from the paid members, will
stand New South Wales, with only about a
million of inhabitants, £33,800 a year, or for
the five colonies of the island an outlay at the
rate of £169,000. The population of Australia
only equals that of a first-class city, and there
is no reason why the public should accept either
Mr. Brown’s or Mr. Abigail’s assessment of
the executive difficulty of managing their
affairs. Let us go in the direction pointed out
by President Jackson, -who, when he opened
Congress, rode to the gate alone on his old white
horse,' and then when he had done his work,
unhitched the horse and unostentatiously rode
away again.

The Course of Justice.
The hideously erratic course of Australian
justice, and the wildlyjuncertain vagaries of
Australian judges, have engaged public atten-
tion for many years, but so far no one has made
any serious effort to find a cure for this social
disease. The establishment „of a Court of
Criminal Appeal is one obvious remedy, but
such a court, if presided over by the same class
of irascible gentlemen who now dispense gallows
and penal servitude on so lavish a scale, would
be only a partial and ineffective antidote at the
best. The occupants of the Bench are mostly
men who are rendered irritable by age and
disease, and senile decay, and they relieve their
sufferings and their aggravated feelings by
hanging the public, or sending it to gaol in a
liberal and generous way which is fast bringing
the law into contempt. Some of them are
harassed by gout, and as the Oriental despot,
when suffering under this complaint, gives vent
to his agony by flaying his doctor and setting
lire to the nearest of his subjects, so the judge
finds expression for his inward grief in passing
life-sentences, interspersed with chains and
solitary confinement. Others are afflicted with
liver-complaint, and the recurring paroxysms
can be traced by the path of devastation which
the patient leaves behind him when he takes his
seat on the Bench, and proceeds to administer the
law with the vigour of a man who would gladly
flog.the whole earth, and have it hanged after-
wards. The judge with a pulmonary disease is
generally mild and humane in the balmy air of
an Australian summer, but in winter he is im-
pervious to reason, and scatters long terms of
imprisonment in a way that rises superior to
law' and evidence and all other mundane con-
siderations ; and his colleague, the judge with
rheumatism, backs him up in his proceedings,
and becomes an irresponsible being at the same
periods, and under the same circumstances. The
judge with corns is uncertain, and administers
the law best in dry weather ; he is governed by
barometrical pressure, and when he orders a
prisoner to undergo twelve months’ gaol for
stealing a jug it is generally a sign that rain
may he expected. As for the legal authority
who suffers from dyspepsia he is liable to break
out at all times and seasons, but after a public en-
tertainment where he generally hangs somebody
if the chance presents itself. And the moral of
this is that no man who is suffering from pain
or disease can be safely entrusted with the life,
or liberty of his fellow-man. The animal that
is in pain generally bites somebody by way of
getting square with the world at large, and
even a judge is only an animal with a large
salary after all. His conduct may be blindly
irrational, but as humanity in general acts
in the same way under the influence of the
same impulse, the result is only what is to be
expected. The individual who i« kicked by a

kicking his inferior, and the latter passes the
injury on till it reaches someone so low down
in the social scale that he can find no one below
him to damage. In the case of the judge who
is infuriated by a pain in his stomach the
prisoner at the bar serves as a convenient
safety-valve, and unfortunately this latter
person has practically no redress. And for
these reasons it should be a sine qua non that
the administrator of the law should be sound in
body as well as in mind, and the race of old,
worn-out, diseased judges, who are soured by
gout, and aggravated by rheumatism, and
biased by brain-clots and partial paralysis,
and are eternally going away on leave, and en-
joying during their valetudinarian holidays,
the wages of at least twenty navvies apiece,
should be promptly abolished. Persons are
hanged often enough that jurymen may dine,
but the necessity of hanging them because the
judge is haunted by the ghosts of the drinks
and cigars which undermined his constitution
when he was young is not distinctly apparent.
Dear Bulletin, — The labour party will never
obtain political power in Australia until they
are organised on the American plan. Fiasco
will follow fiasco until they forget everything
they think they know, and follow the teaching
of experience. The Trades and Labour Council
has no business to meddle with the selection of
candidates at all. It should be the mainspring
of the political machine, and its function should
merely be the determination of the party creed.
The electors themselves should select the candi-
dates. To do this each electorate should
be mapped out and divided topographically
into fifty precincts. Persons appointed by the
Trades and Labour Council should call meetings
of the electors of each precinct, and at each of
these meetings an elector, residing within the
precinct, should be chosen as delegate to the
convention of the electorate. This body, com-
posed of fifty delegates, should then proceed to
select from amongst themselves, by the balloting-
out process, the candidate. The whole thing
works like a machine in America —the party
voting with the utmost solidity for the party-
ticket. I believe firmly in the residence-quali-
fication of candidates. A man’s next-door
neighbours are sure to know him well; and
it is surely an impertinence on the part
of a man to desire to figure as a candi-
date for a constituency if he cannot secure
the appointment of precinct-delegate. They
haven’t got the ballot in America yet; we have,
and I am convinced that the blending of the
American system of selection, with the Aus-
tralian system of election, would place a perfect
electoral weapon in the hands of the Australian
Democracy. If the Trades and Labour Council
like, I will show them how to put four labour
men in for West Sydney, at the general elec-
tion, no wrangling, no heart-burnings, a solid
organized party; and the whole thing should
not cost them more than sixty pounds.—-Yours
truly, J, A. Philp.
Just now philosophers of the cheap or
“ garden ” variety appear much perturbed over
the future of humanity, insoipuch as modern
Civilisation allows no play for natural inter-
sexual select ion and the survival of the fittest.
In the first place it may be observed that the
improvement of the human species is not
evolved by this agency as inbreeding simply
renders its followers increasedly inaccessible to
favourable alteration. Secondly, the concentra-
tion of wealth, intellect, and physical perfection
into a limited minority would not benefit
society but positively effect its deteriora-
tion. The general diffusion of the virtues
of the few among the vices of the many is
the sole logical path towards permanent race
improvement ! Presuming the world to be
divided into two sections, the one comprising a
select aristocracy of highly-developed organisa-
tions, and the other representing a horde of
ignorant, depraved, and deformed specimens,
the inevitable result must naturally follow —
the destruction of the former by the latter, a
period of anarchy and retrogression. No social
reform founded on exclusive lines rather than
absolutely inclusive principles ever spelled any
Other word than eventual self-defeat and failure.

On Saturday, October U, the local courthouse was the
centre of attraction in consequence of the prosecution by
Mr. Mackat, owner of Bulgandranune station, of ten
shearers, who had left his employ when the shearers were
palled opt by the A.S.U. The evidence of Mr Mackay
went to prove that the men had engaged under a verbal
agreement, he having called Ins men up before shearing
commenced and said to theml require you to shear
under last year’s agreement. There are a number of you
here who shore with me last year, and, for the informa-
tion of those who did not, I may sav that the principal
Clause is that under which any man being discharged
before shearing is over will ho paid off at 17s. (id. per 100
sheep instead of £1 per fOO.” The men came and told
him they wanted to leave in consequence of the strike ••
hut he would not consent. The men, in defence, con-
tended that they had a perfect right to leave upon for-
feiting 3s. 6d. per 100. The Bench, however, lined them
£< each, and, in default, imprisonment. The money was
paid forthwith. 4 peculiarity iy connection with the
case is that magistrates were brought from Duhbo—ho
ijniles- to adjudicate on this case, whilst local magistrates
were kept in ignorance of the proceedings, and were,
therefore, unaware of the cases coming on. it is difficult
to understand the reason why four of the defendants weve
in the first place fined £5 each, when, at the instance of
the prosecutor, the fine was raised to £7.
bet us repeat a remark which we have made
fifty times in this connection : The administra-
tion of law in the bush by honorary magistrates,
chiefly representatives of class interest, con-
stitutes as shocking a scandal as did the
periodical rolls-up of honorary city beaks in
the bad old days when amateur J. ’sP. used to
assemble by the score with the deliberate inten-
tion of corrupting and over-ruling the decision
of the stipendiary magistrates.
Premier Gillies says he can’t just now find
time to introduce a one man one vote bill and
that he can make no promises as to the future.
And, says the Argus, anent the Strike division
in flic Victorian Assembly :—“lf this be a low
standard of political morality, we must remem-
ber that in democracies you cannot obtain a
very high one.” The poor Argus actually
tbinks it is living in a Democracy! With
plurality of votes ! ! Let the masses remember
that this is the fundamental question which, if
answered, would at one bound lift them on a
voting level (as to chances) with the classes. The
One-man-one-vote is the Excalibur that is able
to clear all obstructions which now prevent the
people themselves taking possession of their
own legislature. Turning to the Argus’s con-
tented statement that very high standards of
political morality —mark the word !—cannot be
obtained in a democracy, we have heard of a
Lincoln and a Garfield at least, in one demo-
cracy a little more democratic than Victoria’s.
U e suppose the Argus has a lofty assent for
the converse In moneyocracies you cannot
obtain a low standard of political morality.”
I lie mere statement of this antithesis is
sufficient to show how placidly the filthy old
ogre of shoddy ocracy sits atop of her mullock-
heap. It is almost a shame to disturb her state
of blind bliss by heaving a dead cat at her.
Once more the Argus barefacedly endeavours
to bolster up the Capitalistic position by
mendacious sophisms. The particular outrage
committed in the following sentence, i.e. , “No
man, unless a spendthrift or vagabond, need
ever despair of earning a competency and
becoming an employer himself after a salutary
period of probation as an employe,” calls for
immediate refutation for these reasons:—
firstly, the variations of social opportunity
which render it possible for a man who has
amassed a pile to lose the lot owing to the dis-
honesty or bankruptcy of his trustees or
creditors. Secondly, the difference of circum-
stances whereby a man may never be enabled to
raise his head above low-water-mark at any
time owing to ill-luck, bad health, or other
causes. Thirdly, the fact that the earnings of
labour barely suffice to enable any married or
intellectual man to exist upon, while the accre-
tive properties of mechanical contrivance, i.e.,
stored labour or capital, are daily increasing in
productivity and remunerative power while
those of labour are decreasing pari passu..
Given a man totally disabled physically, but
possessed of £IOOO cash, and a labourer with no
other capital except his inherent capacity for
work, it is possible for the former to create an
indefinite amount of wealth for himself therefrom
by the expiration of the year, while the latter can
be only worth a few paltry pounds at the best.
Fourthly, that as civilisation has advanced the
relative award of pure labour in proportion to
the aggregate wealth of the community has
diminished and not increased. The few
shillings a day more wages than those tendered
in past times have not been an equivalent re-
muneration considering the progressive standard
of average comforts. Fifthly and lastly, be-
cause there is no morality in parsimony at all
but a decidedly vicious feature, for the simple
reason that the abstinent man by lessening his
consumption reduces production and thereby
depreciates the collective standard of living,
throwing simultaneously his fellow-workers into
idleness. The mere fact of the existence of
wealth justifies man in availing himself thereof
for consumption. After these remarks have
been well driven into the wooden-head of the
above-named philosophical journal, perhaps it
may not be necessary to continue repeating these
well-worn truisms.

The result of the West Sydney election is
a hopeful sign of the progress of Australian Re-
publicanism. The principal indictment of the
Sydney daily press against A. G. Taylor was the
not over choice way in which he alluded to Queen
Victoria, As the upholding of the Crown is the
first duty of the “loyal” citizen, it is fair to
assume that those electors who voted for Alderman
Taylor are loyal citizens, and that those who
voted for A.G. are disloyal. Imperialists may
take a little temporary consolation from the fact
that the {Kill only represents half the voting-
power of the electorate. But the Republican
answer to this is that loyalists who didn’t vote to
keep out so violent a republican must be a poor
and lukewarm lot.

“ Romeo find .Juliet,” as staged at Sydney
Royal, is chiefly to be commended as a series of
pretty pictures; viewed as a representation of
human life it won’t stand analysis. In
appearance, garb and gesture, Bellew is an ideal
Romeo, lint on first night ho was often inaudible,
and throughout chewed his words as if he enjoyed
them. Weakest when he attempted to depict the
lava-like warmth of an Italian lover, he got guyed
by the gallery in the balcony-scene for the series
of snorts that were intended to shadow forth the
difficulty with which he repressed hij amatory
Vesuvius. In his duel scenes, which had a family
resemblance, the fury, vigour and brevity of
the struggles were the hits of the evening.
In repose Mrs. Brown-Potter would have looked
Juliet had she not made up like a plaster-cast. As
usual, she did her utmost to destroy the mobility
of a very kissable mouth by laying colour thickly
thereon. The fair lady avers that she has never
seen Juliet played ; certainly hers is like no other
impersonation of the part that we have en-
countered, but then it is also unlike anything
under heaven. Had she served an apprenticeship
to her art she might have achieved greatness, but
she does not possess the genius that alone can
compensate for ignorance of technique, lack of
systematic training. It is attention to detail that
makes great impersonations, and Mrs. Potter is
not artificial enough to conceal her artificiality.
Her gestures are usually inappropriate, she is often
tragic when no emotional display is necessary, and
in nearly all her movements she is angular and
spasmodic. The Mercutio of Robert Inman is the
most even impersonation of the cast, and as one
critic asserts that he is too heavy and another
that he is too volatile, he must be just about
right, though we think a little more mercuriality
would be an improvement. Edward Sass plays
the Friar like a phonograph ; the Peter of Poulton
is acceptable as Peters go ; and Charles Westma-
cott, as the furious Tybalt, keeps folks awake
while he is about. Jenny Watt-Tanner is snuffed
out as Lady Capulet, and the Nurse of Mrs.
Edouin Bryer—she has been playing it over twenty
years—is a finished performance. John Brunton’s
scenery is very good.
* * *
As “Called Back,” despite small circles,
is running to bigger business than did “ Macbeth ”
at Sydney Her Majesty’s, it may be said to have
caught on considerably. It has been thought of
late that George Rignold had so fallen into flesh
and heaviness of spirit that it was impossible for
him to elude himself, but as Macari he upsets that
idea, and, acting with brightness and despatch,
gives an impersonation that is, probably, second
only to his Colonel Challis. The Ceneri of Brother
Bill, well-sustained throughout, is chiefly remark-
able for the intensity of his denunciation of the
traitorous Macari and the realism of his death-
scene. Kate Bishop was warmly welcomed back
to the stage, and though now-a days some-
what matronly for parts of this character,
she played, especially in the last act, with
all her old-time vigour. As Gilbert Vaughan,

over lugubrious, but when maudlin sentiment and
small talk (there’s a lot of both in “Called Back”)
were thrown aside, he was himself again ; Arthur
Greenaway was haunted with similar tendencies as
Kenyon, Miss Watts-Phillips makes the most of
Mary Vaughan, and the Anthony March of Harry
Hoyte was snappily and effectively played. Mr.
Clint’s scenery is creditable. By the way, in the
original cast at London Prince of Wales when
Beerbohm Tree played Macari, George Anson was
the Ceneri, Bellew the Gilbert Vaughan and Alice
Lingard the much-ill-used Pauline,

Though “A Pair of Specs.” has done
good business at Sydney Criterion, it will be with*
drawn on Saturday night to make room for “A
Night Off,” that shnekingly funny adaptation
from the German of Von Schonton by Augustin
Daly, produced at Sydney Opera House about
three years ago by an excellent company. On the
present occasion George Anson will impersonate,,
as before, Marcus Brutus Snap, the “ barn-
stormer ; ” Annie Taylor will once more play
Susan; Brough will undertake the Professor,
formerly handled by Maltby; Mrs, Brough will
replace Myra Kemble as Niobe j Cecil Ward will
step into the long-vacated shoes of Titheradge as
Jack Mulberry ; Owen Harris succeeds Hans
Phillips as Damask ; but who will play Zantippe,
the part created by Jenny Watt-Tanner, we
know not.

At Bondi Aquarium last Saturday a strong
gale prevented Mdlle. Viola from taking flight,
and so the seronatty little Australian seventeen-
year-old just walked round and made folks vow
they d conic and sec a little more of her next
Saturday. Jacques Ibbot, probably the fastest
man in Australia, has most suggestively pitched
his tent between the shark-pond and the donkey-
stable, where ho sits at the recoipt of sixpence
with nothing to do but curb bis appetite. He is
a well-developed, athletic young man with a set of
teeth that vainly crave active employment, and
seven doctors have testified that he is thoroughly
sound and in the possession of a magnificent
digestion, which he has for some years kept in
subjection by a strictly fruit and vegetable diet.
He started off on Saturday to live during forty
days on nothing hut filtered water and an occa-
sional hastily-grabbed mosquito. Eatnot—we
should say, Ibbot—weighed 12st. 21b. after his last
dish of apricots, and lost 6sb. during tne nrst Zi
hours. At this rate there will be nothing of him
left but a Saratoga and a full wardrobe at the end
of the period. Flynn, of Virginia, the General and
Lady Mite are in good buckle.
** * *
“ Harvest,” the new and beautiful play at
Melbourne Bijou, is a story of woman’s wrong
extending over a period of twenty years. Its
main idea seems to have been borrowed from
“ Man and Wife,” inasmuch as the prologue shows
how a young husband is inspired by a cynical
army captain to avail himself of a technical acci-
dent in his Scotch marriage with a tall, penniless
angel, in order that he may renounce her for an
amorous widow of boundless wealth, who dies
behind the scenes, leaving the conscienceless
caterpillar a prey to remorse, and a large landed
proprietor to boot. But when he finds old age
creeping o’er him, and the delights of the ballet
have turned to dust and ashes, and the shadow of
his expensive family tombstone darkens his little
bit of soul, why, then the insect drivels about the
unmarried wife he threw off, together with her
blue-eyed boy in the days when he didn’t want
’em.
* # •
The old Tory desire for an heir, one of his
own flesh and blood who will raise the rents when
he is gone, grows upon the old man as his lost
hair begins to come out on the comb. Then the
cynical military person, also become aged and re-
pentant, trots forth some more Scotch law to the
effect that if the other fellow finds the first lady
and marries her in the regular way, and the sight
of Heaven, all may yet be well. Meanwhile, the
deserted mother is waiting round the corner to
reap a harvest of vengeance from her crop of
sorrows, and when her son lias grabbed the flutter-
ing heart of his father’s stepdaughter, and the
father himself proposes to return like the prodigal
to the home of his offspring—that is where harvest-
time comes in. The tale of 20 years ago is un-
folded to slow music, and mamma bids her blue-
eyed boy come out of it forever, so that she may
gloat over an all-round scene of suffering and die
happy. But after a brief revelling in vengeance
the old lady allows her great moral purpose to
dissolve in a flood of tears, and the one aim of her
existence seems to be but vanity after she has hit
the bull’s eye. Womanlike, she has reckoned
without her “ feelings,” and finally takes back to
her breast the insect that stung her, whereupon
every other character in the drama makes for
matrimony.
* * #
Maud Williamson, whose bronchial tubes,
unhappily, trouble her more and more, is power-
ful and picturesque as the unmarried wife. The
part involves a continual overhearing of conversa-
tions and a persistent popping up at critical
moments, but Miss Williamson is a mistress of
broad effect, and never fails to throw life into the
stagiest of situations. Scorn is her trump-card,
and takes a big trick everytime she plays it.
Frank Cates, the husband, is good in the prologue,
but when he affects to develope into a remorseful
ruin the part leaves him astern considerably.
Titheradge is always right as the military friend,
though perhaps he might pile on a little more
penitence or the cool rascality of his advice re
the Scotch marriage. Emma Temple puts in the
prettiest bit of lovemaking she has yet accom-
plished on the Bijou stage, Lyndall is "more than
usually like the good young man who died, Fanny
Enson scores in a minor position. The humour,
which is occasionally strained and artificial, gets
justice from Boucicault and Pattie Browne, and
two of Spong’s scenes are noble works of art.
“ Harvest ” was written by H. Hamilton, who,
judging from his play, should be the coming man
in dramatic authorship.
*■ * * *
The cynic and sceptic, of habit dyspeptic,
The girl who is giddy and gushing,
The dudey young chappie who seldom seems
happy
Unless at a bar he is lushing !
The bald-headed paters who go to the theatres
To leer through their wicked old glasses
When vigorous dances are giving them chances
To study the legs of the lasses.
The dowager fat, in her laces and satin,
The sylph from a shop in the city,
The butcher-boy strapping, whose stamping and
clapping
Produces encores for each ditty ;
The young and the aged, the spliced and engaged,
Or such as are neither at present—
In fact, the whole boiling of idle and toiling
Who think comic operas pleasant,
Will vote it a treat to get into a seat
For this latest of Gilbert and S.’s —
The gay “ Gondoliers, ” and laughter and cheers,
From stalls and from tiers, will ring in one’s ears
Till Christmas appears, or another old year’s
Played out at the Melbourne Princess’s.
* * *
The above is intended as a Gilbertian
expression of belief in a very bright future for
“ The Gondoliers,” an opera which contains eveiy
element of popularity several times over. It givt s
more jokes and music for the money than any
other work of the renowned series. At a first
hearing the librettist’s share in the success seems
greater than Sullivan’s, whose melodies, especially
for the first act, are unusually suggestive of the
Established Church. Tte duets diffuse a strong
religious influence around the house, not to men-
tion secret fears that Williamson or Garner will
presently put his head out of the managerial box
to exclaim : “The Lord loveth a cheerful giver,”
and then come round with the plate. Much of
the music is delightful, however, and the opera
presents a gorgeous aspect throughout. Two
scenes by Phil. Goatcher (the Finn’s new artist)
and Brunton are as handsome as could be, and the
dressing, dancing, and stage-management, in fact,
all things save the cast, carry ad mi ratio' 1 by
storm.
# * *
Elton, Maggie Moore and Howard Vernon
are the only people who thoroughly meet the
requirements of their parts, and those parts are
not the most prominent. Ryley is a fair success
as the baritone gondolier, and Leumane repre-
sents the other one with more vivacity than was
expected, but the best that can be said of them is
that they easily hold their own with such apolo-
gies for operatic “stars” as Ida Osborne and
Flora Graupner. The former lady, although an
intelligent actress and a sweet iiasal warbler,
hasn’t a very paralysing personality on the stage.
Flora Graupner possesses no strong qualification
for the billet beyond a good singing voice, and is
out of place in any part that hasn’t been written
down to her figure. For the rest, pretty Florence
Young doesn’t yet understand how to speak liei
lines, and her amateurish performance in a com-
paratively important character is too painful to
dwell upon. It may be fairly supposed that such
a magnificent stage production has never before
been presented with so little regard for the
strength of its alleged supports.

THE LABOUR QUESTION,
Put out your tires and go to bed,
Is what the voice of Labour said.
The good old times have gone away,
The voice of Labour is here to-day
Eight hours’ work and eight hours' ease
And seek repose just when you please,
Such seems to be the latest rule
That is in vogue in Labour school.
Never mind; be of good cheer
And have your bottle of St. Louis beer
Be happy, mother, son, and daughter,
And drink Sarsaparilla and Iron water
“The Land of the Living” is another
piomising Melbourne show, which will keep the
Alexandra filled until Cup week is dead and done
for. It is a fine, full-flavoured article by Frank
Harvey, with a touch of the supernatural thrown
in to frighten the chief villain. The heroine
dream •> things that were happening in the other
act at the other end of the world, and as she is
relating her vision the parlour looking-glass dis-
closes a bird’s-eye view of darkest Africa, or some-
where, and she hears her husband’s voice singing
ten thousand miles away, which makes the villain
roll his eyes like a Christy minstrel. “Land of
(he Living” can give points to many worse
dramas, all the same, and it suits the Alexandra
down to the ground. There the boys follow the
story by way of the performers rather than of
their particular characters. They know Albert
Norman is the villain even before he rolls his eye,
and when Maggie Knight trips on, looking so
bright and cheerful, the boys heave a sigh for the
sufferings they know have got to come anon.
They likewise understand that Walter Howe is
an indestructible hero whose character is bound
to be cleared at the finish, and their knowledge of
what the future will bring forth for Mr. and Mrs
Bland Holt is founded on a solid rook of past
experience. It is good to be at the Alexandra on
a Saturday night.

** * »
The Palace Hotel, Melbourne, is a pleasant
place for any white citizen of quiet, unassuming
habits. It has been set on fire several times
within the last few weeks, and one night recently,
when business was particularly brisk, it started
into a blaze four times in four separate places.
Sometimes the combustion begins in a room which
is locked up, while the proprietor is sleeping on
top of the key for additional security, and at
other times the disguised’detective who is looking
for a clue goes out of a room to sneeze, and before
lie has got half through the operation the apart-
ment is full of kerosene and conducting itself
like a volcano. Occasionally a visitor takes a bed
at this combustible establishment and locks him-
self in, and plugs up the keyhole, and puts a chest
of drawers against the door, and fastens the
window and ties it up with a rope, and then five
minutes after he has gone to sleep someone builds
a bonfire underneath him and he has to run for
his life, making strong, promiscuous observations
as he goes. A watchman tramps over the pre-
mises all night, but when he goes upstairs some-
one generally fires the cellar, and when he rushes
down to look after it there is a flare-up on the
roof. A report that the incendiary set a light to
the proprietor happily proved unfounded, but he
and the water-tank are almost the only parts of
the building which have entirely escaped, and the
mental wear and tear promise to send everybody
concerned into a premature grave.

Ab the Adelaide Royal, Geo. C. Miln has
been vigorously rattling away at Shakespeare to
business which has been fairly satisfactory. The
tragedian’s best performances were in “Julius
Caesar” and “Richard the III.,” although taken
all round none of the representations can be voted
excellent. This is not wholly Miln’s fault; his
support has been rather tame, Miss Louise Jordan
m particular being thoroughly unsuited for the
parts she has undertaken. G.O. got rather
annoyed at certain local press criticisms at the
commencement of his season, and cleverly put on
the injured air, with the result that he got nothing
but sympathy from both press and public after-
wards.

Th, ere was a terrible row among Gus Harris’s guests in
tne foyer" of Drury Lane on the first night of “A Million
Money.” Proceedings were opened by little James
M Neill Whistler, the eccentric Scotch-Yankee critic and
artist, lustily laying his cane about the shoulders of
Augustus Moore, while vociferating “Hawk! Hawk!”
Moore, a much taller and younger man, soon pushed or
knocked his fiery little foe to the ground, whence he was
raised and led away, jabbering and gesticulating angrily,
by some friends. Then the Irish blood of Horace Lingard
became eager for the fray, and, not satisfied with some-
thing that had appeared in Hawk, he aimed a blow at
Guss3 r , and the two were soon writhing in grips, but were
quickly separated before any damage had been done,
except to Moore’s hat, which was trodden out of shape.
Oisgustus ” is a snobbish person, who evidently requires
kicking badly, but such little runts as Whistler and Lin-
gard were never built for the job.

The Potter-Bellew contingent having given up their
proposed dramatic recital at Adelaide Town Hall in order
not to interfere with a trades-union demonstration, the
local Council passed a resolution which, it is said, has
been forwarded to every head centre in Australasia
directing the unions to handle all goods labelled “ Potter-
Bellew Company ” without demur.
Referring especially to the parsons who went to see
Judah at the Shaftesbury, Spurgeon said, at his Taber-
nacle, on an off-night, that—“ The Christian Church of
the present day had played the harlot beyond any Church
in any age. There were no amusements too vile for her.
Her pastors had filled a theatre of late, and had set their
mark by their clamours on the labours of play-actors.”

May we suggest to Mayor Burdekin the advisability of
removing Sydney’s £15,000 organ to his own back-yard
or any part of his premises where it can be conveniently
stowed away ? The instrument was not, so far as we
know, imported solely for his amusement, and though
the gentleman runs the Government of the country, like-
wise the Opposition, it is not evident that he absolutely
owns the right to throttle an instrument purchased with
public money.
Miss Ernestine Henriques was given a benefit concert
at Sydney Y. M. C. A. Hall the other evening, for the
purpose of raising funds to send her to Europe for the
completion of her musical education.
Says Herbert Standing: “It is, or was, a general rule
never to speak the last word of a play at rehearsals. In a
serious drama, 1 have known William Rignold and many
others, who, having a pathetic or dramatic part to play,
have spoken it correctly until they reached the last two
words, when, instead of completing it according to
‘book,’ they have deliberately turned it off into a jest,
rather than run the consequences of ill luck.”

Back to Graft.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
x’m going back to graft, it’s true, because I guess
we’re done,
Aik l see no sense in keeping on the battle just for
fun ;
There’s che wife to be con-idered —site’s a sticker-
up for “rights,”
But a mother can’t help thinking of the children's
appetites.
Maybe I am faint-hearted, and, perhaps, the
cause ain’t beat
But justice isn’t rent, my boy, and principles
ain’t meat;
The right side licks the wrong side if it only gets
fair play,
And yet how very often does the wrong ’un win
the day !
Our side was right enough, as I’m ready to main-
tain
(Though we stood to lose a lot, mate, and had
nothing much to gain)—
But our line was too extended for the forces at
command,
We must muster in battalions if we’d hope to
make a stand.
’Cause why? That’s just the question which it
doesn’t do to shirk ;
There is always surplus labour as is sneaking
round for work;
We could hold our own with masters, could us
Unions, no fear—
If the men as Avants our billets didn’t skirmish in
the rear.
We may call ’em dirty blacklegs, as I’ve called
’em, I admit —
But when the belly’s empty, Bob, one’s bound to
earn a bit;
We think they ought to join us out of sympathy,
although
It’s only when we're striking that they get a
decent show.
The strong ’tins, Bob, will always shove the weak
’uns to the wall
While Unions ain’t able to provide a job for all;
And this here blackleg labour—which the masters
call it free —
Is a-steadily increasing, mate, as far as I can see
Of course, they’re mostly loafers, with a turn for
