But the kids are short of tucker, so I’m going back
to graft.
E. FISHER.
It is cabled that the London Dockers
“ are now sullen and dogged in their demeanour.”
Carrying two-cwt. bags of salt for sixpence an
hour is just the tiling to make a man look chirpy
A cablegram says that “the Australian
beers shown at the Brewers’ Exhibition, at Isling-
ton, are much admired.'” Out in Australia we
don't admire beer. We simply drink it and keep
our opinion to ourselves.
It is cabled that the London dock com-
mittee announces its intention of offering the
dockers fair wages, with the right to pensions, and
will undertake to engage labourers without the
intervention of middlemen. It is marvellous to
watch what a lot these poor non-dividend-paying
dock-companies find they can do for the men now
that the latter have shown their teeth.
A vessel laden principally with beer went
down lately at the mouth of the Swan River and
the dreary city of Perth foresaw a cheap drunk
such as no inhabitant ever drank before. Every
man who could raise a boat was out fishing for
beer, and people who had no boats swam and
dived in search of beer, and fat men who couldn’t
swim went forth on rafts and gates and planks
and everything else that would float, and dragged
the beer off the bosom of the heaving deep. And
then, just when the drunk was fairly in progress,
the police came down with an intimation that
beer found drifting round at sea was the property
of the Crown, and so the grass withered, and the
Mower faded, and the sunshine vanished away,
fur the Queen has confiscated all the liquor that is
left. Evidently Her Gracious has an eye for any-
thing that is drinkable, but the next time a
wreck takes place at the Swan River, Victoria by
the grace of God may do salvage-duty herself.
Her loyal subjects are feeling tired.
You really ought to drink Wolfe’s Schnapps.

One of two Americans staying in London
earned seven pounds weekly during his visit, by
betting his companion per arrangement made
before arrival, one pound that it would rain each
day. He drew his money before noon on each
occasion.

The Australian larrikin is, after all, but a
poor, ignorant savage as compared with his cousin
in dear old Hingland. “ Scuttling” is an art of
which the larrikin knows absolutely nothing. On
calm Sabbath evenings he is content to recline
against a verandah-post and curse creation between
his dark brown spits, or perchance he has enough
spirit of enterprise to kick a Chinaman, or heave
’arf a brick at a lame dawg— but no more. Mean-
while the rorty boys of Britain are venting their
older civilization in the “ scuttle,” a Sunday diver-
sion which radiates from the busy Manchester
district. There they have “ scuttling” clubs
for mutual improvement and the common weal.
Five or six members form themselves into a pic-
nic, and at a given signal whip out their clasp-
knives, and rush down the main thoroughfares
jabbing their weapons into the community at
large, who shriek furiously for the perlioe. Re-
cently a merry party of five “ scuttlers ” were
actually arrested by the Manchester constabulary,
and a dull, humorless Recorder inflicted severe
pains and penalties upon the quintette with a view
to the suppression of this healthful Hinglish
pastime. Evidence unfavourable to scuttling was
given by several mutilated citizens, two of whom
had lost an eye during the festivities, while one
was smarting under the indignity of a large gash
in the.interior. Scuttling is expected to go on
and prosper despite these proceedings, however,
and the comparatively apathetic larrikin of this-
country may hide his diminished head until he
learns to tighten the silken bonds by roaming
around promiscuously with somebody’s bright blue
eye on the point of his trusty blade.

A Remarkable Story.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The student of psychical phenomena may
find in the following strange story some elements
worthy of consideration.
It was a sultry evening in January. In the par-
lour of a neat little cottage that stood, surrounded
by shrubbery, in a quiet Melbourne suburb, there
sat a party of four, to wit, Brown and wife —the
owners of the house—and two old friends, named
Smith and Jones. Brown was one of the lucky
few that came out right during the great Bendigo
mining boom of 70. He realised a competence,
got married, and had retired into private life in
the spot where we now find him. On the other
hand, Smith and Jones were not so fortunate, and
were still adventurers. They had been extremely
unlucky for a long time, and their joint wealth
was now represented by a solitary ten-pound note.
But if they had little cash, they had an inex-
haustible fund of self reliance and internal
resource—a fair equivalent. Moreover, they had
been many years together, had gone through the
vicissitudes common to Bohemians the world over,
and were equal to either fortune, so that their
case was not so bad as it seemed. They had just
comedown from Bendigo, en route for Gipps Land,
and had broken their journey in order to spend a
few days with their friends, the Browns. Brown
and Jones, having mystical predilections, the four
were engaged on the night in question in a seance.
They had passed the initial stage and were now
thoroughly en rapport with the spirits, ten of
whom had signified their presence in the orthodox
manner. The raps did not come from the room,
but from various parts of the room, difficult to
locate; and Smith, who was sceptical, and attri-
buted their origin to physical causes, desiring to
ascertain if they were accompanied by any vibra-
tion, kneeled down and put his ear to the wainscot.
Just then a vivid flash of lightning lit up the
room, a deafening valley of thunderclaps followed,
startling the whole party. A, flickering light, like
a will-o’-the-wisp, hovered around Jones for a
second or two, and then disappeared. Owing to
the oppressive heat of the night the doors and
windows had been left open, but as the rain was
now falling, and the air was much cooler, Brown
rose and closed them, while his wife lighted the
lamps. Whereupon it was discovered that Jones,
who was a delicate man, extremely susceptible to
external influences, was in a cataleptic fit, or
trance. The Browns, much alarmed, wished to
send for a doctor, but Smith assured them that
it was not necessary, averring that the fit would
soon pass off, when Jones would bo none the
worse. This proved true. In half-an-hour Jones
was himself again, but as he complained of a
slight headache, and it was getting late, the party
broke up and retired for the night.
# * #
On the following day the two friends
walked into the city, when Jones proposed that
they should return immediately to Bendigo,
Smith, much surprised by his friend’s sudden
change of mind, demanded an explanation. There-
upon Jones informed him that during his trance
of the previous evening he had seen a rich patch
of gold lying exposed on the open roadway in
Blank-street in the city, at a given point
which they both knew well. At first, Smith
very naturally received this communication as a
joke, but when Jones declared his fixed deter-
mination to go and -see for himself, he hardly
knew what to say. The whole thing seemed too
ridiculous to talk about; but, nevertheless, after
exhausting all his powers of dissuasion, and find-
ing Jones still immovable, Smith finally consented,
under protest, to accompany him in his pursuit of
this phantasm, stipulating only thatnothing should
be said of it to the Browns,
In the course of the day, Jones succeeded in
procuring a poncho, after some trouble—for such
garments were things of the past—and in proof of
his faith he purchased a small stout leather bag to
oarry the gold ! And that evening’s Bendigo train
parried with it Jones and Smith, bound upon
what might fairly be styled the most insane
errand ever undertaken by sane men in this
prosaic age,
» * •
As the train rolled into Bendigo at
midnight, a heavy storm was passing over the
city, and the rain falling in torrents. Getting
into a cab, the friends were driven to the Sham-
rock Hotel, where they immediately went to bed,
and Smith was very soon fast asleep. As for
Jones, his overstrung nerves and morbid condition,
of mind put sleep out of the question. He lay
tossing about till daybreak, when he arose, and
after bathing his feverish temples, donned his
new poncho, an,d taking his bag under hia arm,
went downstairs, and was let out by the hall-
porter.
♦ *. •
The rain had long since ceased, but
the sky was still gloomy and overcast, there was
no one astir, and the city streets looked weird in
their emptiness. The constable on his beat stayed
for a moment his rhythmical tramp, and looked
reflectively after the draped figure hurrying by.
Jones moved rapidly on, his thoughts insensibly
coloured by the depressing outlook before him.
As he turned into Blank-street a slaughterman’s
cart dashed past, taking beef to the butchers in
the cool of the early morn ; throwing showers of
mud in. every direction, and awaking the echoes
of the silent streets in its rattling course. By h:s
aide ran, the freshly cut wheel-tracks, stretching
out in front, direct for the goal of his hopes.
Over at the station—no distance away—an engine
was getting up steam ; and as he glanced from,
side to sidp, at the shops and houses on, either
hand, the manifest incongruity between his in-
tensely realistic surroundings, and the ridiculous
character of his mission, sent a chill to his heart.
And all of a sudden, the tremendous absurdity of
his anticipations came upon him with the force of
a revelation. Greatly disconcerted by this new
aspect of things, and filled with misgivings, his
pace slackened in unison. As he slowly ap-
proached, with faltering steps, the point indi-
cated in his vision, a clock hard by struck “V.’
* * *
Just here, there was a considerable in-
cline, and the great thunderstorm of the previous
night having swollen the waterways enormously,
the rushing torrent had torn up the road in its
course, leaving behind a number of deep furrows
and water-pools. Beside one of these, elose to
the slaughterman’s wheel-tracks, Jones suddenly
stopped, mirabile dicta, there at his feet lay the
treasure-trove of his trance! There it lay, all
round the margin of the hole. Coarse gold and
fine ; nuggets of all shapes and sizes were there
washed nearly clean by the waters. Heavens!
What a sight ! So profoundly affected was he by
this marvellous realisation of his dream, that a
faintness came over him, his limbs shook, and he
feared he would fall; but this feeling quickly
passed off. He glanced nervously around him;
not a soul was to bo seen. Gathering up his
poncho he crouched down, and with eager, trem-
bling hands scooped into his bag fistfuls of the
dull yellow metal; half mud, of course, but what
of that? Handful after handful he gathered |
then, when he had secured the major portion of
the gold, the clang of a closing door warned him
that people were stirring. Desiring to avoid
observation, and not being very avaricious, he
determined to leave the remainder for the benefit
of the next comer. He arose, his mind in a tumult
and concealing the bag under his poncho hastened
hack to the Shamrock, where, awakening Smith,
he triumphantly displayed to his friend’s dumb-
founded gaze his wonderful golden harvest.
A PORTER'S ROMANCE.
At door of big Kawphy Pallis.
COUNTRY MAIDEN (giggling): “MOTHER, THAT’S SUCH A POLITE NAVAL
OFFICER STOPPING HERE. EVERY TIME I COME DOWNSTAIRS
HE OPENS THE DOOR FOR ME WITH SUCH A SMILE. AND, DO
YOU KNOW (blushing), I THINK HE WANTS TO PROPOSE
TO ME,”
MOTHER (gazing fondly at her offspring). “I SHOULDN’T WONDER AT ALL,
MY DEAR. IF HE DID.”
Meanwhile the lucky “next-comer”
proved to be a butcher’s boy driving some sheep
to lijs master’s shop in the city. He passed close
by the hole, and his quick eye perceiving the gold)
Which remained lying scattered around, squatted
down and began picking it up. He was very soon
joined by a chum, and whilst the two boys were thus-
agreeably engaged), a man came along, who, seeing
there was a good tiling on, ran, for a shovel and’
dish, and cleaning out the hole carefully, panned
out a good many ounces.
The local papers of the following day stated
that the man and two boys netted over a hundred
pounds worth of gold between them. Not so
bad, before breakfast. Upon a careful examina-
tion of the ground it became evident that it had
been the site of an. old camp, and that the gold
was a “plant ” buried by the owner for safety, in
the early days, of the field when murder and!
robbery were common. The workmen, in forming
the road, had gone within a few inches of it, and
the rushing waters had done the rest.
• # #
Row much. Jones netted! the veracious
chronicler knoweth not. But he and Smith may
no longer be classed as _ adventurers ; being, at
the present time of writing, comfortably settled
on a snug little property of their own. in “sunny
New South Wales.” They often visit their friends
the Browns, and many a seance the four have held;
since that fateful, one of the trance. -Jonas has.
had no. more visions ; but, as might be expected,
he plumes himself not a little on the happy results
of the last, averring that “second-sight runs in
the family.” As for Smith, his scepticism is com-
pletely routed, and to suoh an extent has his res-
Eect increased for all things occult, that he declares
is readiness to accompany Jones on a like expe-
dition whenever he desires, to the uttermost ends
of the earth, if need be.
J. LYALL.
A Death Strike.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
In the year 188— I was surgeon-superintendeht of
an Australian benevolent asylum, and had under
my care some six or eight hundred old and indigent
men. They necessarily were recruited from the
poorer working-classes, though occasionally some
who had occupied far different positions came
under our notice. I now narrate one of the many
strange incidents of asylum life of which I was an
interested spectator.
The winter of the year mentioned had been Very 7
wet and severe, all the country around was more
or less flooded, and the aVerage daily mortality of
the poor old inmates, through the dampness and
inclemency 7 of the weather, had been very high.
The cemetery where the dead were buried was
situated about half-a-mile from the Asylum, in
rather low ground, and great difficulty had been
experienced even in former years in keeping it free
from soak age. This year, however, was such an
exceptionally damp- one that towards the end of
the winter the cemetery was practically sub-
merged, the- coffins having to be held down and
the earth piled' quickly on them to prevent them
from floating.
One morning one of the wardsmen of the Asylum
informed me that a deputation of the old men
were waiting to see me.
Poor, battered, old waifs 1 It was a pitiful
sight. The drift from the seething, bubbling*
restless sea of life —what heart-stirring tales their
seamed and wrinkled faces told !’ Their ages
ranged from- GO to 90> years, and the dozen that
stood before mo represented how many noble-
hopes, ambitions, and aspirations—doomed, alas,
never to be realised and to end —oh, how miser-
ably ?
“ We’re wishin’ to* spake to yer banner,” blurts
out old Mick. “ llech mon,” says Donald
Maolvor, “eanna .ye baud ye tongue, didna’ ye a’
agree that it’s me that’s to speak to the doctor ?”
“ Well, doctor, we’re wishful to say that we’re
sore disirest at the state of the burying-ground,
and we have come to ask you if it cannot be
changed’. To think that our bodies will lie soakin’
and soakin’ till the Day of Judgment is more than
a good Christian body can put up with and we
have decided’ that we will not die nor be buried in
such a-water-hole.”
Donald always spoke the broadest Scotch if
excited, and I have omitted most of his collo-
quialisms.
Having been assured by mo that the mattei
would be attended to, the deputation departed
my ears catching, amid the retreating noise of the
shuffling feet, “ Ye’re right, Donald, we—refuse
—die—Tim —Flanigan—floatin—t-h-e-r-e— y-e-t.”
I had the place drained, but all to no purpose
and soon after wrote off to obtain authority to use
another piece of ground. The reply giving the
necessary consent of the authorities without which
interments could not take place, was not received
for three days, and, strange to relate, not a single
death occurred during that time among the old
men.
I received an answer on the tmrd day, ahd im.
mediately informed the old fellows of the new
arrangement, and from that day the average daily
mortality among them Was maintained, and they
died off in peace.
I should state that up to cnis there had ne vet-
been less t han two deaths per diem in the Asylum;
and they ranged sometimes as high as five or six, so
that the three days’ death-strike was unparalleled
in the history of the institution, has never been
known to occur since, and is, furthermore, the
only strike ever recorded in its peaceful anhals.
RICHARD VINCENT.
It is stated that the insurance losses by
reason of the great Sydney fire are so great that
they cover five years’ premiums on all Sydney
property insurances.
Capt. Edie, of the U.S.S. Co.’s fleet, is to
succeed Capt. Brown as Shipping-Master at Syd-
ney. Capt. Edie has a first-class record, and is
one of the most popular captains who have ever
commanded a ship in these waters.
Judging from their recent speeches, it
would seem that Messrs. Carington, Hopetoun
and Kintore are under the impression that they
a phenomenal trio, and were sent out by way of
making ‘ ‘ a new departure ” in the art of colonial
governorship. At least they state unanimously
that they are “a new departure,” and what in
thunder they are driving at no one knows except-
ing themselves. It is true that Oarington is
going to depart, and that the wives of Hopetoun
and Kintore have departed already : but if this
is the cause of all the row the subject might as
well be dropped.
The latest game of the Melbourne capi-
talists is the formation of an association to induce
British and Irish labourers and artisans to emi-
grate to Victoria “on terms.” If they’re
“good” and “respectful” for long enough, the
cost of their passages is to be repaid them. The
Argus is making a big mouthful in accepting
custody of subscriptions towards the new
the first two or three donations received being
cheques for £lOOO each. The senders of these are
so far keeping their identity dark. The new
strategy may look brilliant to the monopolist-
employers, but we are content to let them flap
their pinions how they please. Socialism cannot
be stayed by all the forces of united classdom.

The world’s greatest orator, Senor Caste -
lar, is about to marry Madame de Riete, the
daughter of Princess Letitia Buonaparte, The
lady has buried three husbands, one of whom
was Rattazzi, a Prime-Minister to Victor
Emmanuel, Her father was English, her mother
a Buonaparte, afid the three dear-departeds, or
dears departed, were German, Italian and Spanish

Very Rev. Anglican Dean Macartney,
of Melbourne, is the one living link with the
last Irish Parliament. It is possible there may be
other sons of members of the last Irish Parliament
still in the land of the living, but DeanMacartney
stands alone in the distinction of having been born
(in 1799) the year before the Union, whilst that
Parliament Was still in existence. His father,
Sir John Macartney, like Sir John Parnell, the
ancestor of the present Irish leader, voted at every
stage m the Irish House of Commons against the
extinction of the Irish Parliament,
Mr, George Higinbotham, Chief Justice
of Victoria, was born in Dublin in 1827, and is
therefore 63 years old. He was a reporter on the
London Morning Chronicle in 1849 ; in 1853 1m
was called to the Bar, and the next year he sailed
for Victoria, He edited the Argus for three
years, but subsequently returned to the laW.
After a stormy political career, during which he
Was ever on the side of Radicalism, he retired
from politics, and when he accepted the Chief
Justiceship (at £3500 annually) his professional
incoire was something like £12,000 per annum.

A hard-up Britisn peer has just acquired
£20,000 cash. Some years ago the son and heir
of Lord Northampton successfully borrowed
£lO,OOO from an insurance company, and by way
of security he insured his life for £34,000. He
never paid any premiums,, however, so the com-
pany paid them aud added them to the debt.
Also, he declined to pay the interest, and it was
also added to the debt—in fact, he consistently
refused to pay anything. When the bill had rim
to £14,000 he gave up the transaction and died,
and then his intelligent father forcibly collected
the balance of the policy, and a noble British
family is consequently £30,000 ahead. Also, a
badly-astonished company pays £20,000 for the
privilege of lending the deceased heir £lO,OOO
without interest, and insuring his life at its own
expense ever since the year 1879 !
Bavousky writes that divorce is an un-
avoidable incident in human life, because the ideal
at 25 is the exact opposite of the ideal at 40.
The greatest curiosity at Versailles is a
mirror, in the glass of which Marie Antoinette,
in the height of her prosperity, once saw herself
with her head off. If you approach the mirror
now, at a certain angle, you see the same effect,
caused by the peculiar lighting.

Sir Sam. Griffith purposes dividing
Queensland into three provincial councils with
full powers of Government in local matters. No
use—full Separation it must be. Provincialism

Gladstone pronounces for One Man One
Vote, but warns the working classes that they
must not worry Parliament too much. The
Labour Parliament of the globe is turning the
flanks of the Palaveraments, in not one of which
is the workman represented.
South Australia has decided on free edu-
cation. In Victoria, State education is absolutely
free and absolutely secular. In N.S.W. it is only
compulsory, which is unjust, considering that it is
there neither secular nor free.
Ex-Treasurer Burns, (N. S. W.), has just
said that he was “never afraid to meet his con-
stituents.” Yet after 25 years’ service he ran
away from the Hunter, apparently, because there
were a few hundred coal-miners on the roll.
Any parliamentary candidate who appears
on the hustings drunk should be locked up for six
months and have his deposit forfeited. To attend
the Supreme Court while drunk means sometimes
a year’s gaol. To appear drunk as a parliamentary
candidate is to commit contempt of the people.

THE GOVERNMENT OF WESTERN AUSTRALIA,
formation of a new ministry.
(rEctke’s telEgram.]
Brisbane, Friday.
The new Ministry has been formed, of which Sir S. W
Griffiths is Premier.
So much for England’s interest in her kawlinies.
It is proposed in the N.S.W, Parliament
that the Premier’s salary be raised from £2OOO to
£3500, and that of several other Ministers from
£l5OO to £2600 and from £l5OO to £2OOO ; also, that
the Agent-General get £5OOO annually. Either
the country is paying the Civil Service too much
or it is paying its chief political servants far too
little. The N.S.W. Chief Railway Commissioner
gets £3OOO yearly and his subordinates £l5OO, and
the Chief Justice gqts £3500, or £l5OO a year mow
than the Premier,
Said Thos. H, Green, the historian, when
a youth Fools'.talk at Oxford of its (the for-
mation of a rifle corps) being desirable, in order
that the gentry may keep down the chartists in
the possible contingency of a rising. I should like
to learn the use of the arm, that I might be able
to desert to the people, if it came to suck a pass. 5 '
And, by the way, what do the Australian labour-
organisations intend to do in the matter of having
workmen represented in the volunteer forces by a
due proportion of officers ?
Mr. Lakeraan, M.L.A., suddenly arose in
the N.S.W. Assembly last week and wanted to
know what the Governor meant by “giving per-
mission ”to the House to elect its Speaker ? The
Premier loftily replied that the formality “ had
been the practice in the English Parliament for
200 years,” The sentiment of this is right against
Young Australian opinion, which asserts that
instead of Parliament asking permission from the
figurehead, the figurehead will have to ask per-
mission from Parliament.
Cursing from the hustings and road-metal-
throwing at elections are only survivals of Aus-
tralian lag-time. They are things calculated to
damn the cause of the sober workman., and are
eagerly seized upon by his enemies as a means of
linking him and his cause with the gaol-bird and
the loafing drunkard. The armed forces of the
country could be put to no better use than to
uphold the right of free speech, by dragooning the
ruffians who throw filth., and missiles dangerous
to life, at candidates for Parliament.
Said Frank Cotton’s weekly paper :
In the present conflict the electors of West Sydney have
the pleasing option of being defeated by the accumulated
property votes behind Mr, John Taylor, or disgraced by
the return of Mr. A. G. Taylor. To say that the unionists
have any prospect of being represented is to insult the
good sense of every man who has any sense ot self-
respect.
This is a very fair statement of a condition of
affairs which makes us doubt the possibility of
regenerating Parliament. If men of the Taylor
stamp are the best that can be induced to come
forward as candidates for the suffrages of one cf
the premier constituencies -of N.S. Wales, how
can one expect studious, intellectual, well-behaved,
sober men to be returned for the outlying con-
stituencies ?
N.S. Wales, in (at the instance of The
Bulletin) devoting Treasury money to the de-
fence of capitally-arraigned prisoners, has again
got ahead of England
When (remarks Labouchere) a prosecutor is too poor to
pay counsel to prosecute, the State retains counsel for
him. Why, then, should it not do the same for prisoners
who are too poor to pay for their own defence ? Surely
the State is not more interested in obtaining the convic-
tion of criminals than in preventing the conviction of in-
nocent men ?
That is what The Bulletin has argued for years.
And now that the question has been satisfactorily
disposed of, so far as N.S. Wales is concerned, let
ns propound the next question which arises on the
road to legal socialism—“ Why should a man who
is simply accused of and has not yet been found
guilty of a crime be put into an iron cage to take
his trial?” We do not think that the fact of a
man being placed behind spikes like a wild and
dangerous beast ranch influences the mind of an
experienced presiding judge, who has so often
seen men cage-tried whom he felt to be innocent.
But the jury, less accustomed to the forms of
criminal law, are invariably prejudiced by the en-
oagement of the prisoner at the bar.

Lady Jersey on her arrival will find the
coast absolutely clear. Not another Governor's
wife will be visible in Australia.
* * *
On the Collins-street block are shown
group-photos of the Hopetoun entourage, with
the principals smirking in the midst. The star
beauty nowadays is decidedly Capt. de Moleyns,
who is pointed at all day with long-handled Lady
Teazle parasols at a safe distance. He has a very
stagey left eye, one of the sort that looks round a
Toorak corner. He is a distinguished amateur
theatrical, and people vainly hoped to see him ere
his departure in “Delicate Ground” and “Out on
the Sly.”
# * #
Lady Jersey, it is cabled, is suffering from
typhoid-fever, and Jersey cables his apologies for
his detention in England. The intelligent Aus-
tralian public gives him indefinite leave. It is
Only interested in the lady.
# * *
Some time ago The Bulletin commented
on the fact that Gov. Kintore had re-named the
township of Innanimcka “Hopetoun,” thus re-
jecting the native name. The S. A. people are
firm about the absurdity of the new name, and,
led by the Reoikter, still call the place by its old
appellation. All Australians would do well to
stick to the same principle in future and thus put
an end to the present system in a practical way.
Those who would bestow old-world names (such as
“ Sandhurst ” for Bendigo) upon Australian
localities can have no belief in the future import-
ance of this country among the nations. A few
years since a vulgar little cockney local coterie
sought to efface the old historic name of Manly
Beach and substitute therefor “ Brighton,” and a
systematic attempt is being made to re-christen .
the townships on the Parramatta River after
places abutting on the Thames. In this con-
nection, The Bulletin hopes one day to see a
rational name bestowed on “N.S. Wales,” and
with a view to helping on a cognate reform, now
alludes to Maoriland for the very last time as
‘New Zealand. ’
# * *
A few years ago, the inhabitants of the
“ Tight Little Island ” having good reason to be
ashamed of certain associations inseparably con-
nected with the name “Van Dieman’s Land,”
altered it to “Tasmania.” It has now been dis-
covered that Tasmania anagrammatised is “ I am
Satan.” They can’t get away from him anyhow.
* * *
A small family difficulty has broken out in
an aristocratic household of the Far West. Some
few months ago a new slavey came out to the
station, and the son and heir fell a victim to the
charms of this artist in hash. She had a com-
plexion of milk and roses, and she could sit down
on her hair when she was tired, and her arms were
round and white, and in addition to all these
things she wore a smile that broke the young man
up altogether. He wrote 28 letters to her with a
promise of marriage in each one, and then his
mother found it out and raised a whirlwind. But
when she had screamed herself hoarse she had to
come down from her pedestal and argue the point
with the goddess of hash, and the lowest price the
goddess would take for the correspondence was
£5OO. She got the money at last, and then went
straight on with the proceedings as if nothing had
happened. She has got 37 more letters from the
young man now, and unless she gets £BOO for them
the marriage will take place in a few days. Woman
js a most unreliable animal.

and descend to carrying a banner V
* * #
Sydney Echo is persistently attempting
to name the big new local street “Jersey Avenue!”
Isn’t it a little too early to grovel to a man whom
none of us have ever seen and whom nobody
knows ? If the Jersey family must be deferred to
in the matter, surely, so far as notoriety is con-
cerned, it is Lady Jersey who claims considera-
tion.

are now very short of funds and that it is almost
certain that the strikers have spent £200,000 in

would have maintained them and their wives and
families for over six months. Mr. Clark and his
friends can’t get over their idea that tea always
means virtue and never dyspepsia and that the

where, is the fortune I have saved on cigars ?”
# * *
There is always a variety
Of scandal in society,
Which helps to make life bearable,
And saves us from “ ennui
Flagrant swindles on our courses,
Gentle whispers of divorces,
While each “ Toorak darling wonders,
Whom the—co. —will really be,”
Curiosity scarce masking,
Ladies one another asking,
“ Have you heard the latest scandal ?”
“Oh ! do tell us more, dear, please !”
Then they say, in accents torrid,
“Oh, it’s positively horrid !”
(They, of course, know nothing worse
Than the conservatory-squeeze ?)
* * #
It is said to have been just like this :
Suite Lothario who accompanied the Governor of
Barataria, one cold night snugly ensconced him-
self in the sanctum sanctorum of Turfito. Turfito
came home unexpectedly—midnight-dragged the
Suite out of bed, in his shirt, and chased him
down street, with a horsewhip. A cabman took
pity on poor Suite, and gave him an overcoat, in
which, over his shirt, Suite staggered to the
Baratarian gubernatorial palace, fell down faint-
ing in the corridor, and stayed in bed for six
solid weeks.
# * *
Colonel Price is being formally considered
and investigated by a “Board of Inquiry,” partly
because of his alleged longing to fire low and kill
somebody, and chiefly oh aofcbunt of the needlessly
emphatic language in which that command is said
to have been expressed. As the board on Colonel
Price consists of an Imperial major-general, a
naval officer and a P.M., The Bulletin rises to
state in advance that Price will be discharged
without a stain on his official character, and the
proceedings will close with a testimonial.

A most distinguished Tasmanian personage
has presented to the local hospital two battered
nitiepenny dolls. One lias lost a leg, the other
suffers from haemorrhage of sawdust. They have
been placed in the surgical ward for treatment.

the so-called “Men of Mark” victims should it
happen that the law makes them pay up. The

getting himself written up deserves to be practi-
cally regarded as a “mark” for all the greasers
in the land.

* * *
The Bulletin sincerely sympathises with
Mr. T. B. Gibbs, a man who, during a long busi-
ness career, has earned the respect of his employes
and the community, in the position in which he is
placed as chief witness at the inquest concerning
the great Sydney fire. Mr. Gibbs is a man of
much energy, who, persistently dogged by ter-
rible luck, has ever bravely shown a bold front to
the world. We dare say Mr. Gibbs would have
been quite ready to give privately to the insur-
ance-offices and authorities, even on oath, all
necessary information concerning his private
affairs, but it is rough indeed that a man with a
square name should have to stand up in open
Court and (Under penalty of attaching to himself
an imputation that he had a lot to conceal) be
compelled to answer, in an enquiry ostensibly
referring to the direct cause of a tire, every ques-
tion asked him in the course of what in lawyers’
phrase is termed “ a fishing expedition.”

A Chinaman was sued in a Victorian court
the other day for the price of a dress-suit which
he had bought from the local tailor. The Chow in
evening dress ! This is the last straw that breaks
the dude’s staylaee.
* # #
Governor Hopetoun states that he has an
engagement on hand for every day of the next
three months. That is to say he has got to feed
at a banquet, when he could feed finite as well at
home j or else he has to open a bridge, or a barn,
or a public building which would serve its purpose
quite as well if there was no official opening at
all; and in between these important duties he has
to go to some place which has so far managed to
crowd through without him, and make a dead and
exhausted speech about nothing. A Governor’s
life is an arduous one nowadays. So is the life of
the Indian fakir who undertakes td stand for a
quarter of a century or more on one leg and it is
quite as intellectual.

“ Why,” asks John A’Dreams (James
Runoiman), “does the British male say ‘My Dear’
to the wife of his boßom when he hates her most
direly ? and why does the British female return
the compliment when she only regards the male
as a trapped animal whom she will skin alive until
his life-policy enables her to skin him dead?'
And then John goes on “to give the real though'
behind the nauseous verbal caress :
To “My Dear.”
Your face is mean ; your vulgar moutn,
Primed with a long-lipped feline sneer,
Seems aching with perpetual drouth—
A drouth of whine, and snarl, and jeer.
The muddy reaches of your soul
Are strewn with rotten wrecks cf lies ;
The slimy currents foully roll;
Their surface glitters through your eyss
You sanctimonious cockatrice!
Were all the pulpit’s babble true,
I’d steep my soul in cankered vice
For fear of sharing Heaven with you.
You stab ray heart with dastard speech J
You slay the new-horn waifs of good ;
All hell alone had power to teach
Your callous, flaunting hardihood.
A poet once lived in my heart,
And made me glad for many a year ;
You did not kill him with a dart,
You smothered him in filth, My Dear.
All ! dear, dead poet, rise again,
And make the joyous choirs resound,
Your song should cheer the sons of men—
Alas ! He testers underground.
And pray, what does all this mean ? Simply that
John finds marriage prosaic.

to Secretary Knox, of Broken-hill Proprietary

sioner for New South Wales at the Mining

# # #
Mackennal, the Australian native sculptor
who is shortly to return to Europe, has executed,
among other trifles, a clay profile of Mrs. Brown-
Potter. Speaking of his model he says; “Mrs,
P. is a most artistic woman, and never tires of
giving sittings. She used to sit for days together
that I might get the tone and expression of her
La Toscan smile and commit it correctly to the
materials.” This valuable smile, therefore, must
by this time have become pretty well worn out.

One of the Melbourne and Sydney
curiosities is the bush bride, just arrived with
Iter bridegroom, off in a hurry to the Kawphy
Pallis. They flaunt the thing, insult all the
people in the street with their happiness ; the
bride is so wonderfully new, even to her boots,
Once a just-hitched bush couple from Warrack-
nabeal or Bungeeltap, spent their happy evening
in the Speaker’s Gallery of the Melbourne Legis-
lative Assembly. 4 4 Tastes differ, ”as the man said
who kissed his cow.

A True Bill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JoNEs and Robinson lately essayed to ‘ ‘dri ve a coach-
and-four” through the N.S.W, Crown Lands Act.
Brown and Jones are neighbouring squatters out
West, their runs are contiguous, and they hate one
another like poison. Brown is a big man, even for
the West, Jones is only “ small potatoes ” in that
region, but anywhere else his run would be con-
sidered big enough. One day Brown, alluding to
the size, or rather want of size, of Jones’ run,
called the latter a “ boss cocky.” Gall and worm-
wood ! This to a real live squatter ! The soul
of Jones was at once possessed with ideas of
revenge.
About this time Jones fell in with Robinson ; I
cannot describe to you the business of this last-
mentioned better than by saying I d.n’t know
what he isn’t or hasn’t been ; I could tap him on
the shoulder within five minutes’ walk of Sydney
G.P.0., but beyond this I will not particularise.
J Siting on Robinson’s advice, Jones decided to
take up a number of homestead leases on Brown’s
resumed area, and a vague sort of treaty was entered
into. Things had to be vague at this stage, because
under tHe penal clauses of the N.S.W. Land Act
anyone inducing anyone else to be a dummy, or
illegally to take up a homestead lease is liable to a
term of imprisonment with hard labour not less
than six months and not exceeding two years.
Jones was to find the dummies and take up five
blocks of 10,240 acres each. Robinson was to find
the sheep and would, when the homestead leases
Were granted, finance matters so that the improve-
ments would be paid for. J ones hied him back
to bis station, and about a month afterwards five
were taken up on Brown’s resumed area,
the applicants being a stockman, a horse-breaker,
a gardener, a boundary-rider, and a nondescript—
all in the employ of J ones.
“Dashed unneighbourly thing to do,” said all
the western squatters, and Jones was boycotted
by them from that day forth.
“But his troubles had only just begun, he had to
send these five men into town to lodge their ap-
plications at the Lands Office. This necessitated
five sums of £42 13s. 4d. each being planked with
the Crown Lauds Agent, and five “sprees” and
their incidental expenses; the immediate result
being that Jones’ solicitor had to bail out of the
look-up two of the applicants next morning•
finally, they were got back to work, but in a
month or two’s time the applicants had to attend
before the Land Board again, which meant five
more sprees, and Jobes had to part with five more
cheques before his “ dummies” would return to
duty.
Meanwhile Robibsoli had sebt ob to Jones’ own
run the sheep that Were to be depastured on the
homestead leases as soon as the necessary pre-
liminaries had been arranged, and they naturally
required a well-watered paddock to depasture in,
a paddock Well worth £l2O a inobth, as time- go
out West. By this time the survey fees on the
lease applications were due and had to be paid.
They varied from £l5 to £25 on each—say £lOO in
all. Jones paid this and thought it high time he
saw after the “ financing ” proposed by Robinson,
as the improvements, amounting in all to nearly
£2OOO on the five leases, had to be paid within 90
days from gazettal.
The virtuous Robinson, at this stage, wanted ab
agreement signed that practically involved Jones
into admitting that he was an employer of dum-
mies. This the latter declined to sign, and the
former demanded then a lump sum to be “ bought
out.”
“There’s nothibg to buy you out of,” said
Jones.
“I’ll get the leases cancelled,” replied Robin-
son. “ I’ll see Brunker and tell him they’re all
dummies.” And the pair parted wrathfully.
So far Jobes’ ordinary banking-account had
carried him along well enough, but when it came
to finding a couple of thou, for improvements, the
assistance of a finance company had to be called in.
Before their row Robinson had introduced him
to the managers of a couple of these institutions,
and to one of these gentlemen Jones now betook
himself. “ Five homestead lessees wanted certain
advances, they were prepared to mortgage, &c.,
&c., the leases had already been gazetted, the
improvements consisted of so-and-so, and with
expenses, amounted to £l9B0 —would the company
advance it?”
“Well, you see,” said the manager, “Mr.
Robinson has been here and he has notified us
that he was interested with you in this matter,
but as he found reason to doubt the bona-jules of
these applicants he has withdrawn. Of course,
my dear sir, I do not imagine there is anything
wrong with them, but my directors, you know,
have to be consulted, and I am afraid that we
cannot oblige you, Mr. Jones.”
A similar reply was received from the manager
of .the other institution to which he applied, and
after vainly carting his proposals all over Sydney
Jones returned West a sadder and a wiser man.
When the 90 days elapsed the leases fell in
because the improvements were unpaid, and
Brown laughs in his sleeve whenever anyone talks
of homesteading his resumed area. Robinson
took his sheep away from Jones’ place, declining
to pay one penny for their depasturing, and the
latter didn’t dare “Court’’him over the matter
lest the foregoing incidents should come to light.
Homestead-dummying isn’t always cakes and
ale. Jolies’experience in this cost him well nigh
£4OO, and there is not a madder man out West.
Wolfe’s Schnapps—lt is grand.
An enterprising Sydney firm lias published
a large sheet containing 28 pictures of the R.O»
hierarchy of Australasia, the central figure being,
of course, that of Cardinal Moran. As a large
number of the faithful run pubs., the picture-
runners have invaded every whisky-mill bearing
an Irish name, only to find that the owner’s
veneration for the Cardinal as a churchman is
swallowed up in his disgust at the teetotal prin-
ciples of his Eminence. “ The Kyardinal’s prach-
ing, ye see, takes the bread out of our mouths,
and we have no money to buy his likeness wid.”

milking it are the land-jobbers, judges, lawyers

“ Once upon a time,” says Arnold White,
writing on food-adulteration, “there were three
flies. They were common flies, who inflicted the
same annoyance on the persons who occupied the
house as others of their race. But these three
flies were as devoted friends as Athos, Porthos,
and Aramis. One clay the three friends started
for a journey in the noontide heat in search of
something to eat. In due time they came to a
loaf of bread. One of the flies, ravenous’ with
hunger, settled on the bread in advance of his
friends. Alum was in the bread. The digestion
of the fly was unfitted to grapple with alum, and
he shortly died in great agony from spasmodic
contraction of the coats of his stomach. Oppressed
with grief, but still hungry, the two survivors,
who were spared by the impetuous appetite of
their dead friend, continued to search for food. A
bowl of sugar came in sight, of which the younger
of the two eagerly availed himself. But,
alas! the sugar was prepared with sulphuric
acid, and again a hapless insect was done
to death. Desolate and sad, the survivor
reviewed the situation, found he had nothing left
to live for, and resolved on putting an end to his
existence. He looked around for some instru-
ment by which he could leave a world that had no
further interest for him. Spying a fly-paper, he
resolutely plunged his proboscis into the lethal
surface, and resigned himself to death, with his
wings modestly folded around him. But the fly-
paper was adulterated, and he found, instead of
death, a healthy and stimulating meal.”
The Tip From Down Below.

j.t was in the stilly night,
As I lay in bed a-thinking,
That a spectre white and “ tight *
(He had certainly been drinking)
Staggered through my bedroom door—
Eor a door can’t stop a vision—

With an air of calm decision

Don’t you see I’m very sleepy ?”
But he didn’t go away,
He squattered there and sniggered ;
I knew not what to say,
So hazarded : “I’m jiggered !”
Which was certainly the case,
Yet a shorter word and ruder
Had been scarcely out of place;
Then this horrible intruder

On a system too erratic —
Had him up at half-past four.
When the animal’s rheumatic I
He'll win you back your stuff
Some day, I shouldn’t wonde?
But he isn’t good enough
Till he tucks his hind legs under,
And moves likes a machine
Never troubled with lumbago—*
And I wouldn’t trust the Queen,
Not with any of my sago.
“There’s a fellow down below
Who has put his little gal on
(He’s a masher, doncherknow)
Some gloves about Tantallon ;
But you'll find the heavy weight's
What I used to.call a ‘real ’os,’
And he’ll beat both stable mates,
Aye,, and get in front of Melos.
You can back old Carbine, rare,
For a place, and he’ll annex it j
But I sniff the morning air,
So had better make my exit.”
The spectre said “Good-bye,”
And quite Mephistophelean
Was the twinkle in his eye
When he added : “Back Chaldean j
Lot me your Mentor be,
As once I was aforetime;
I’d tell you all, you see,
But I haven’t any more time.”
Then he vanished through the door,
Which was locked, there’s no gainsaying
And I haven’t seen him more,
Though I seem to hear him saying—
C arbihe, when the Gup is won,
H orse of horses shall be reckoned,
A 11 his rivals, save but one
L eft again behind the second;
D own the straight he fights with fata,
H quine hero colossean,
A nwin, with his lighter weight,
N abs him on the dark Chaldean.

In West Australia (rather indefinite, this)
a publican has living with him a (Queensland black
gin who, arrayed in clean moleskins and a coloured
shirt, entertains his guests. She gallons round on
the back of a buck-jumper which she handles with
consummate grace, and when she drops into town
with a few friends she walks into one of the bars
and says in a most manly way, “ What are you
going to have, chaps?” The lady is universally re-
garded as a perfect treasure.

In this issue is an advertisement of Messrs.
Caldwell and Company, of Melbourne and Lake
Albert Vineyard, Wagga Wagga. The business
of this firm, which is one of the oldest and most
extensive in Australian wines, was about two
years ago, during the “boom ” time, floated into
a limited liability company, but finding it
was not less successful under the management
of directors the old firm recently repurchased it,
intend carrying it on under the original style and
title of Caldwell and Company, and now solicit the
patronage of all their old clients.

The wife of a Governor of a British colony
has just started for Europe, escorted, on the first
stages of her journey, by the co-respondent in a
recently-squashed divorce-case. She couldn’t help
it, unfortunately. Pretty nearly every imported
official has been a co-respondent, or ought to have
been one, at some period in his career, and some
of them have been two or three co-respondents,
and had a half-share in an elopement. The same
remark applies to the locally-grown official, and to
a good many other people as well. These obser-
vations are not intended as a severe reflection on
anybody.

The literary wags of Great Britain still
write funny little things about the sea serpent.
Until one of these well-authenticated marine eels
gets out of his natural waters, and comes waggling
along the Thames as far as London Bridge, the
cockney humorist will have his annual joke. It is
enough for him that neither he nor any member of
his family ever saw a sea-serpent, therefore they
don’t exist. But for his having applied spermaceti
to his cut finger the cheap London cynic wouldn't
believe in the whale —a ten times more remarkable
monster than any sea-snake that has yet come
within view of people who were not suffering from
jim-jams at the time.

rjlO THEIR CREDIT BE IT SAID.
That Australians generally are not slow to
recognise a genuinely good article—whatever form
it may take —is a fairly well established fact. Nor
does it redound less to their credit that whenever
special merit is perceived by them they are not
slower in freely acknowledging it. and giving it
such support as may lay in their power. The
Waterbury Watch Company receives from day to
day dozens of testimonials from wearers of their
watches in all parts of the colonies—a fact very
gratifying to their endeavours to place reliable
timekeepers at a price within the reach of all.
The following are samples recently received :
Mr. Thomas G. M'Guire writes from Nyngan,
N.S.W. ; “I am well pleased with the Water-
bury as a good, cheap watch, costing very little
for repair. If I had taken as much care of the
Waterbury as I have taken Over other v atches, it
would not have had to be repaired so soon. How-
ever, it has acted admirably under the circum-
stances, and pleased me very much.”
Chas. .1. N. Horstey, Esq., J.P., C.P.S.,
Mudgee, N.S.W., writes under date: “I have
much pleasure in stating that my Waterbury
watch is, without excep ion, one of the best
watches I have ever owned, although I have had
many more expensive. It is a reliable and
excellent timekeeper.”
CAUTION. Intending purchasers of the
genuine article should note that all watches manu-
factured by the Wn“wbury Watch Co. have the
word “WATERBURY ” printed in fine type on
the dial-face, and also the interlaced letters
“ W. W.C0.,” without which none are genuine.

The Referee.
The Melbourne Stakes this year is likely to
prove a public trial for the Cup, as it lias proved on so many
previous occasions, and the prophet who, after selecting six
horses per week for six weeks, can chip in one last grand
absolute final tip on the day before the race should cer-
tainly contrive to fall across the winner or something
“awful near it.” At time of writing, however, “the
great coining event ” casts very little shadow before. The
man who essays to write wise things must evolve them
from his inner consciousness and other sources that are
far less reliable than well-run weigh t-for-age races like the
Stakes and Derby. Even the betting-market gives no
hint as to what may be expected from certain
“divisions,” nor, for the matter of that, have some of the
said divisions fixed up private matters to their satisfac-
tion as this sermon is compiled. Looking at matteis
from a precedent point of view, The Bulletin would
recommend the placed horses in the Melbourne Stakes as
good place investments for the £lO,OOO handicap, always
presuming that the “cracks” are brought out for and
appear to do their best in the weigh t-for-age race on
Saturday.
« * #
. Meantime, seeing that every second man in this
Hemisphere has something to say about the Cup, it is
permissible for The Bulletin to, metaphorically speak-
ing, buttonhole the guileless punter and tell him a tew
things which it “ knows,” silspects, or guesses, without
prejudice to the solid editorial opinion that nobody can
possibly know much until the winning number goes up.
We will, at any rate, avoid the exasperating imbecility of
selecting every horse in turn, in order that we may ulti-
mately stand upon our head to announce that this
journal said Thingummy was sure to happen if 15 others
didn’t beat it. Commencing at the top of the list, then,
let us take Carbine to beat Melos and all the horses
weighted at from Bst. upwards. Melos seems to be
more generally fancied than Mr. Wallace’s great horse,
but no good reasons for the fancy are apparent.
Sixteen times the two horses have met and thirteen times
has Carbine won, giving his old opponent 121bs. over two
miles on two occasions. Carbine had a bad heel when he
ran in last year’s Cup, yet he finished in front of Melos,
who meets him again this year upon exactly similar
terms. Excuses were made for Mr. Gannon’s horse, it is
true, but excuses are weak things to swear by. We prefer
to swear by the facts that Carbine has won his last ten
races clean off the reel, and has beaten Melos the last six
times they have met. What is there to show’ that the one
has improved more than the other since last Cup day?
# * *
People do say that Hickenbotham will run first
and second for the Cup with Tantallon and Prince Consort,
and the stable is credibly reported to be sweet upqn the
former. But neither horse has been directly tried with
Carbine, and w’e have some grounds for stating that the
trainer doubts their ability to beat the champion at the
weights when he, a sluggard at exercise, conies to put his
best leg foremost. Ramage says he never yet crossed a
horse in any way approaching Carbine as a racing-
maonine
* * #
Probably the Cup will go to Ballarat, where so
many good stakes are likely to go this season. Vengeance,
Chaldean, Enuc, and Annesley are the most notable Cup
horses trained in that mysterious district, hut the latter
is not certain to run, even if he doesn’t earn a few' pounds
extra by winning the Derby. Enuc, to our way of think-
ing, is worth backing in preference to Vengeance, and we
will take him to heat all the other three-year-olds in
the race. The horse is bred to stay, and has been
tried to stay the two-mile course* and his third
in the Caulfield Cup will bear criticism. Chaldean
on the other hand, has no recent form, nor any form for
two years past to recommend him, save his apparently
brilliant run in the Caulfield Cup of ’SO, which run was
suddenly interrupted by his breaking a blood vessel. The
horse has been for some time in charge of the Wilson who
trained Sheet Anchor and Bravo, and some who are not
fools say that he is fit, and a “good thing” for the Cup
which his sire, Assyrian, won eight years aim. The bet-
ting-market will point to the better of Chaldean and
Enuc, although they are not in the same ownership
Perhaps the dark horse may show for the Melbourne
a takes, and so enable punters to judge his chance by as
niueh as they can see with their own eyes. Anyway
either Chaldean or Enuc is certain to he a favourite pro-
bably first favourite, on the morning of the big event, and
those who “ follow the money ” will stand a fair chance of
oemg on the absolute winner at last.
* * * #
Oddly enough, the four-year-olds don’t look
particularly dangerous, Muriel and Chintz have fair
onanoes on last year’s form, which is more trustworthy
than the two-year-old form of Marvel, who cannot be
worth backing at anything less than twenty to one. We
•naked out that Chintz, if a favourite bn the day is
iikeiy to heat everything but Carbine and the pick
Between Enuc and Chaldean. Benzon is a horse that con-
unually threatens danger without doing any mischief
aka y ueen oallnot be good enough on later performances,’
ana uoyalstone, in the opinion of good judges, will not
wjth a boy on his hack. Of nil the lighter
weighted old horses we fancy Eniani, simply because a
“lever hookmaking crowd are going for it, and the man
witn a sovereign to throw away upon an outsider might
no worse than give Silvermiiie’s half-brother a show,

# # *
The “knowing ones” who estimate horses’
chances for the Melbourne Cup by the quantity of “ long,
strong work ” rubbed into them week after week before
the race, may be interested to hear that Memoir, the
winner of the Doncaster St. Leger, was discarded by turf
prophets on account of her preparation having been
interrupted for several days. It was the interruption
that ultimately pulled the mare through.
» * •
What show have the general public of dropping
upon a winner in a large field when even the trainers of
the stables do not know the qualities of the horses under
their own care ? Take Megaphone, for instance. Hicken-
botham, apparently, did not know' that the colt had
“gone off,” when the dollars were piled on him at Caul-
Held. It would be hard to find a more astute horseman
than William Kelso, but even he, when sending two horses
for a race, does not plank his “ hit” on the right animal.
At Warwick Farm, a couple of weeks ago, Kelso sent
Velocity and Southerly Buster - the latter was backed,
but Velocity won. On Saturday, at Moorefield, he sent
Velocity and Dolphin. The stable pinned their faith and
planted their coin on Velocity, but again they were mis-
taken, and the winner turned up in Dolphin.
* * *
The heavy backing of Titan for the Melbourne
Derby is about the most extraordinary piece of work in
connection with the coming Victorian meeting. Heavily
backed for the Sydney Derby and Caulfield Guineas, he
went under, and to an outsider his three-year-old running
does not show that he has a million to one chance of
winning. On “ gallops ” Gatling should about win, and
no win would be more popular with the crowd than that
of the unlucky owner of first-class and second place
animals, Mr. “ Bill ” Gannon.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, —A paragraph in your “Referee’
column on right-handed racing-tracks recalls to my mind
an incident. When a lad I was present at a Warrnambool
(Vic.) race-meeting. A steeplechase was one of the items.
A friend of mine (some years older tha nyself) and I
were w’atching the steeple being run. M 3 ! end stood to
win a good sum on a local horse, ant' ’ onsequently
w'atched the champion, Lone Hand, vt anxiously.
Suddenlj 7 J exclaimed: “B} 7 Jove, i.J s baulked;
Corrigan will never get him around now.” I looked upon
my friend at that time as a great authority upon all
matters connected with horse-racing, and therefore asked
for an explanation. He said : “Lone Hand would never
go around this course, as it is a right-hand one.” Lone
Hand had baulked at the double just past the entrance,
and so suddenly did he stop that Corrigan went over
first. If I remember right!} 7 , the horse did not finish in
that race. I do not know 7 wdiether my friend’s opinion
about the right-handed course and Lone Hand w 7 as correct
or not. He afterwards proved, poor fellow, to be not
altogether right in his cranial department, for he is now 7
a member of Parliament and is married.—Bex The
Trapper.
* * #
The writer of “ Sporting Notions ” in the
Referee (Lon.) says : —“ I recollect not many years ago a
M.0.C., or Zingari, or Gentlemen of England, or some-
thing of that sort, side against the Australians, and that
on tire last afternoon, when a draw was inevitable, the
Zingari, or M.C.C., or Gentlemen of England amused
themselves by chucking up balls for Bonnor to hit.
This egregious burlesque of cricket was counted as
a factor in the first-class averages, and the cabled details
of the batsman’s performance caused great admiration
and excitement on the other side of the globe.” The
cable liar has always held a brief for Australian cricket.
* # #
The Warwicks had quite a pleasant little outing
on Saturday last, when they met the Carltons on Sydney
Association Ground. Donnan and Bannerman, of the
Carltons, w 7 ent first to the wickets and were not separated
until 281 runs were made, Donnan being run out for 117.
The game now stands one wicket for 296, W'ith Bannerman
not out 131, and Marr not out for 33.

Larry Foley used to say that he never had a
pupil who could equal Slavin in “ barrack ” and “ skite.”
The latter is certainly putting on a little too much frill
in London.
* # #
•Toe Thompson and Alf. Jacobs are ab ut to
establish a racecourse in San Francisco and settle down
there. If they were followed by a thousand or so of the
profession Australia would not suffer. Chapel-bazaars
w r ould fully supply the national taste for gambling.
* * #
The Sydney peds. have formed a union. The
peds. in their adversity have nothing to blame except
their own “cronk” transactions. The gaffers and pro-
moters can now obtain better sport from the novices. So
can the public who pay the piper.
* * *
It is amusing to see how a certain sporting
scribe, when_ his selections do not come off—and that is
often—invariably writes next day about the “glorious
uncertainty of the turf.”
* # *
A Sydney gentleman (!) is advertising for ladies
to form a cricket club “ similar to that already formed in
Melbourne and in the leading counties of England.”
* * #
Steeplechaser Bushman, over whom there has
been more blaspheming by the general public than was
even heard on the West Sydney hustings, has gone into a
stable which will turn him out a winner if lie has it in
him. He has been purchased by Mr. William Kelso.
* * #
The Rev. Walter Fellowes (of Melb.), whom
Bishop Moorhouse induced to give up cricket, is credited
with the biggest hit ever made at Oxford, when, in 1850,
he hit a hall, howled by Rogers, 175 yards from pitch to
fall.
# * *
An analysis of the Seventh Australian Eleven
shows the Australians made 10,573 runs as against the
Britishers’ 0,739, the average per wicket being Australia
16’525, England 15'409. Of the colonial batsmen, veteran
“ Billy” Murdoch tops the batting average with 23'38,
and then they run : Barrett, 22-50 ; Trott, 19-57 ; Lyons’
17'54 ; Blackham, 15 7 38 ; Turner, 15-10 ; Ferris, 15-2
Charlton, 14-30 ; Gregory, 12-28 ; Jones, 12-4 ; Burn, 10-5 ;
Walters, 10-2; Tremble, 8"6. Trott and Murdoch each
twice scored over 100 in a single innings. Trott 186
against Cambridge, and 102 against Gloucestershire ; and
Murdoch 158 against Sussex, and 129 versus Cambridge.
Among the howlers Turner took the cake with an average
of 12-45 for 7303 balls, whilst Ferris bowled 8405 for an
average of 13-43. Turner clean bowled 103 wickets to
Ferris’s 59.

The Australian Athletic Club, it is said, intend
to give a £2OOO purse for competition among the heavy-
weights-Jackson, Goddard, Perry, and Ryan. Probably
Dooley’s name is not mentioned because of his connection
wit h the other bruising-shop, but as a boxer he is inferior
to none, and would apparently have beaten Goddard when
they last met had he not lost the use of his send-’em-to-
sleep paw. If the club arranges this contest on the each-
man-meets-all-lhe-other-men principle, they ought to get
six fairly good goes for the money.

No. XVI.-A Bathurst Field Day.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN .]
“By the Lord! Snowlee, did you ever
hear such stuff as this ?” And the speaker, bluff
Tom Coxson, of Coxson’s Bully Barm—a tiny
little freehold of some twenty thousand acres
watered by Campbell’s River —quoted, in sten
torian sing-song—
When shall the sojis of Bathurst Plains,
With many a nymph and many a maid,
The village queens, and Albion’s swains,
Draw up their bands along the elude V
No more the clanging chain shall sound,
And crime shall hide her ’minished head;
Simplicity shall smile around,
And pity weep o’er sorrows tied !
“ Now, can you imagine such arrant nonsense
being allowed to be printed not to say' written—■
by the authorities? Why', it’s enough to lead to
a break-out on every' holding in the colony !”
To emphasise the disgust Mr. Coxson felt at the
charming vision called up by the poetic driveller
of the New South Wales Magazine, he threw
the old copy of the interesting periodical from
which ho had been reading into (he street.
“Faugh! Here, Mrs. Dillon, give me another
rum to wash the taste out of my mouth !”
Mrs. Dillon, of the Red Cow Inn, Bathurst,
predecessor of the Mrs. Blacks and the Charley
Turners who have successively swayed the
destinies of the “chief hotel in the place, sir,”
hurried to obey the order. Had it been any other
than Coxson who had cast her book into the road-
way she would have called Ned the groom to
throw h m out. She was never tired of reading
those same verses which had so irritated her hot-
tempered customer, and she had kept the maga-
zine till it was stale with time and with beer. For
she had a soft heart and an eye given to dropping
sympathetic tears, though she was hostess of the
R d Cow ; and she really looked forward to the
ia. py days when the May-pole and the dancing-
tent should be hoisted on Bathurst Green, in the
place where now the new springing grass grew
reddish, because the soil was dewed weekly with
showers of blood.
But Tom Coxson was a regular visitor to the
Red Cow, and a thirsty visitor and a generous
visitor, and Mrs. Dillon could not afford to offend
him. When (as regularly happened once a month)
he drove into Bathurst 10 or 12 assigned servants
to receive their usual dose of medicine, he put
them all up as well as himself at the principal
hostelry, and fed ’em well and drank ’em well
both before and after they had interviewed the
doctor. In view of which fact Mrs. Dillon did
not call upon her groom’s services. She did not
even remonstrate with the person who had treated
with such despisement the outpourings of the
muse of her (avourite poet, one Henry Ha Horan
(a Grateful Sovereign decorated him, many years
afterwards, with a C.M.G.-ship). But she jotted
down another line or two to Mr. Coxson’s score,
and in that way healed her wounded feelings.
# # #
Thomas Coxsuii and James Snowlee,
Esquires, land-owners and Justices of the Peace,
had come in to the City of the Plains to join with
two other gentlemen, similarly endowed by For-
tune and the Kings Majesty, to adjudicate at the
ordinary Court of Petty Sessions to be holden on
the morning of Monday, ill st August, 183—.
Having breakfasted very comfortably, they were
now sitting un the verandah of the inn waiting
the opening of the Court.
Snowlee, of Derndale, a thin-faced, small-sized,
watery-eyed man, was deep in the previous week’s
i <sne of the Gazette when interrupted by Coxson.
People said he lived on Gazettes. He read
nothing besides the Gazette, except for its rela-
tion to the Gazette. He was a peripatetic index
to that liveliest and most sensational of publica-
tions, and his mental qualities were stamped with
its characteristics. His ordinary conversation
was’ marked with all the graceful elegance of
Impounding Notices, while the eloquence of a
Proclamation, or an announcement of the reward
for an absconder invariably inspired his magis-
terial dicta. Everybody, from the Governor to
tlie newest of his assigned men, laughed at him
for tins weakness. However, he let the “free”
class laugh and he flogged the other when he found
them out. And by means of this amiable weak-
ness he built up one of the greatest fortunes ever
amas cd in Western New' South Wales,
* * *
It would take a volume of the Gazette of
the compass of the present year’s to record the
eases where he made big money out of his know*
ledge of Government advertisements. Did a
traveller pass Derndale station —the road out
West ran by it —Mr. Snowlee, by virtue of his
magistracy, had him stopped, demanded his
“pass,” and had his “ marks ” examined. Woe
betide the man, whether “ free ” or “ bond,” if he
had marks upon him or his horse that were, or
could be made with a little alteration, identical
with marks ascribed in the Gazette to an
absconder or an offender, or to a stolen horse!
Snowlee would arrest the man on suspicion. If
the fellow had no money Snowlee would have him
brought before the next court, would have him
convicted of absconding or theft, sentence him, or
commit him, as the ease might be—and claim the
reward which, never less I lian v 2 for a man, went
sometimes as high as £5O for a horse. If the man
possessed money, Snowlee would lock him up in
an outhouse overnight. Unto the prisoner would
come, at nine or ten o’clock, not Sn wlee but
Snowlee’s confidential man. Asa “kindly act”
Mr. Snowlee’s confidential man would assist the
| prisoner to escape.
If the prisoner was really an absconder or other
man ou whom Justice —no, that s a slip ; we mean
the Authorities- had put a price (and in one year
in sunny New South N\ ales Authority put a price
upon two thousand heads) the chances were that
he would take advantage of the “kindly pro-
posal ” and bolt, leaving his money behind. On
the other hand, if he were not justly accused, he
might resolve to fight it out. And a strong fight
he would have to make of it, in very sooth, if he
wished to save his cash and his freedom. A very
lucrative business did Mr. Snowlee make of his
justiceship and his recollection of Gazette Re-
ward Notices combined; but bis revenue from
this source was a mere billy-full out of the two-
gallon-bucket of his income. .
Was a land-grant deed in the counties ot
Bathurst and Roxburgh announced as ready years
after it ought to have been issued ? Mr. Snowlee
had an assignment of the grantee’s interest
made, perhaps, the very day the Gazette reached
Derndale. . ,
Was there a tender accepted from amanagamsn
whom Mr. Snowlee “ knew something? ’’ A very
handsome percentage went into Mr. Snowlee s
pocket—that is, supposing Mr. Snowlee could non
see his way to work the contract himself at a
larger profit. .
