Deaf Forty Years.
The description of a simple remedy, by which a
complete cure of deafness and noises in the head of
FORTY years’ STANDING has just been effected,
will be sent free to anyone who applies to
J. H. NICHOLSON,
176 William Street, Melbourne.
impounding notice to suit a friend, before sale-
day ho generally heard from Mr. Snowlee, and
was glad to get off lightly by a communication
with Mr. Snowlee’s “confidential man,” m which
an order on Prosper de Mestre or George Terry,
or a cheque on the Commercial Bank was pro-
minently referred to.
Did a neighbouring squatter still hold a prisoner
to “assigned labour ” some time after the man’s
“pardon” or “ticket” had been gazetted —and
forgetfulness of this sort was quite a common
thing on the part of certain Western land-owners!
—how eager (according to his own statement) was
Mr. Snowlee to pay ten pounds out of his own
pocket to his own “confidential man” to stop
“further bother,” and with what a deprecating
friendliness of tone he would inform the forgetful
squatter that he had done so—“ You see, B ,
I had to shut his mouth !” and how modestly he
would pouch Mr. B ’s ten pounds, and how
reluctantly he would hand his “ confidential man”
a solitary half-sovereign as his share of the spoil!
Well might people say Mr. James Snowlee,
J.P., lived on the Gazette, and very properly
did Bishop Barker remark, three decades later,
when preaching Mr. Snowlee’s funeral sermon,
that, “while the reading of the dear departed
brotner had not been of a wide or discursive
character, it had been eminently profitable. ”
# # #
It was this GrAZBTTE-nourished gentleman
who had been addressed by Mr. Tom Coxson, and
who borrowed the previous Thursday’s issue to
respond to Mr. Coxson’s remarks.
“I quite agree with you,” he said. “There
should be a law in that case made and provided.
Such a document as you have just quoted is an
incitement to mutiny and to an overt and covert
disrespect on the part of the legally-convicted
prisoners of the Crown towards the constituted
authorities. To express any opinion that any
imaginary state of things can be possibly better
than the present state of things is to implicitly
and explicitly assert that the last mentioned state
of things is not the best possible state of things. ”
“ Do you mean to say, Mr. Snowlee,” broke in
a stalwart grazier who had come into the verandah
While the other was speaking, “ that you consider
the present condition of things here as right and
good ? ”
“Oh, is that you, Suchor? That’s what I do
mean. The authorities would alter anything that’s
Wrong. If anything is not altered, then it’s right
and proper. Don)t you agree with me, Coxson ? ”
“On my soul I clo ! ” said bluff Tom. “ And
that’s why I said all these d fellows who
scribble about a time when the chains shan’t clank
and the whip shan’t flog should be put down—
they create discontent in prisoners’ minds, and
make ’em think that there’s a lot of things that
are wrong.”
Manly George Suchor made no further remark,
but turned away when Coxson spoke. The Squire
of Baulkham Hills had no objection to a contro-
versy with Snowlee but he would not argue with
the other. The Campbell’s River man invariably
lost his temper if opposed in argument, and as he
could not well vent his spleen on his opponent,
he would gratify his anger by more severely
punishing his convict servants. Suchor had a
vivid recollection of having worsted Coxson in a
bar-parlour debate some months before, on which
occasion Sam had taken his assigned servant from
the buggy in which he was sitting preparatory to
driving his master home: had “made a court”
by asking a brother-magistrate to preside with
him; and had given the unfortunate man a
“ dozen.”
“ What’s this for, Mr. Coxson?” the convict had
asked.
“ Insubordination,” Sam had roared, “ I’ll
teach you to laugh at me when Suchor takes me
down.”
“Please sir,” the unoffending wretch had
whimpered, “ I didn’t hear no talk, and I didn’t
laugh.”
“You didn’t hear it and you didn’t laugh,
didn’t you? Well if you had heard it, you’d have
laughed, so, constable, take him away, and tell
the scourge to give him twenty-five sweet, and to
look sharp about it, as I want to start home-
wards.”
Mr. George Suchor recalled this incident, and
also that he had reported it to the Colonial Secre-
tary and been politely snubbed for his pains.
And, remembering these things, he walked away
from Ooxson’s company. He didn’t wish to make
the “dozen,” which some poor devil there was
waiting to receive, into a “canary.”
As he retired, Coxson said: “That’s one of
the fellows who spoil the Gov’ment men. Why,
they’ll cut his throat some day, see if they don’t.
There’s only one way to keep ’em in hand—give
’em a dose of medicine regular, eh, Snowlee ?”
And he shook himself in a snappish digust.
* # *
The ticket-of-leave man who served his
Majesty King William IY. as Bathurst Court-
keeper, here crossed the road and signified that the
local Temple of Justice was waiting the presence
of the two magistrates to open its doors —and the
shoulders of its brace of scourgers.
Let us enter with Messrs. Coxson and Snowlee.
We will not, however, pass with them to the
Bench where two other justices—mere nonentities
in the roster of brutality, and requiring, there-
fore, no other label than the names of Smith and
Jones—are already 'seated. No; we will take a
seat by Mr. George Suchor in the body of the
court, and watch the proceedings. But we shall
not note all the oases. Our readers’ appetite for
horrors is not insatiable.
* « •
Mr. Justice Coxson began business, and
without getting down from the bench he said :
“ I have nine of my fellows in, gentlemen, to-day,
for insubordination and laziness, and one for
larceny. If you don’t mind, we’ll take the nine
first, so they can get their medicine and be ready
by the time I want to start. I think we’ll have
to give the other fellow three months, so we’ll
take his case last.”
“As you please, Mr. Coxson,” assented Mr.
Snowlee.
“ Certainly, Mr. Coxson,” said Messrs. Smith
and Jones, in chorus.
And the nine were called. One of them, a
lowering-visaged scoundrel, took off his jacket as
he was hustled behind the bars.
Mr. Coxson descended from the tribunal of
Justice and entered the witness-box. He did not
wait for the oath to be administered by the Court,
but mumbled the form himself.
“I suppose I needn’t go into all particulars,
your worships,” he said. “It’s the same old
trouble. When they’re not insubordinate they
are lazy, and when they’re not lazy they are
rebellious and disobedient.”
“Oh, you needn’t trouble, Mr. Coxson,” said
Mr. Snowlee. “ What punishment will meet the
offences ? The statutory maximum, or shall we
be lenient, as is admissible, you know, by regula-
tion providing for the contingency of the master
not pressing for severe punishment ?”
“I’ll leave it to you, gentlemen,” magnanimously
rejoined Mr. Coxson.
“Have you anything to say, prisoners?” was
the bench’s inquiry.
“Wot’s th’ use o’ sayin’ anythink?” said the
ruffian with his jacket off. “We know as th’
master thinks it’s time for our monthly dose o’
med’sun, an’ we’ve got to swaller it any’ow, so
th’ sooner we get it down th’ better, and th’ longer
it’ll be till th’ nex’ dose.’’
“ You’ll oblige me by giving this man a hundred
for his insolence now, your worships,” said the
prosecutor, “in addition to the punishment for
his insubordination. I will prefer the charge of
larceny against him later.”
“Oh, certainly, Mr. Coxson,” replied Mr.
Snowlee, who registered the decision —two hun-
dred lashes in all.
(Mr. Suchor, in the body of the court, pulled out
a pocket book and noted therein “200.”)
“All right, yer Washups,” said the offender.
“ I knew I was in for it, an’ thet’s w’y I tuk my
coat orf afore I kem in. ”
“Please the Bench,” said prosecutor Coxson,
“I have to apologise for this man’s disrespect to
the court in appearing without his jacket. I
didn’t notice it before. Suppose you give, him
another “dozen” to teach him not to show con-
tempt to the King’s Justice?”
“Oh, certainly, Mr. Coxson,” replied Mr.
Snowlee. (And Mr. Suchor noted another “25.”)
Without a dissentient expression from the eight,
each was sentenced to a “ dozen.” (Mr. Suchor
noted “8 x 25 - 200.”)
The Court had been sitting ten minutes when
Mr. Coxson, having done the great part of his
share of the day’s work of prosecution, replaced
Mr. Snowlee on the bench, and Mr. Snowlee took
Mr, Coxson’s place
# * #
Mr. Subwleeprosecuted four of his assigned
men for laziness and refusing work. The quar-
tette were typical convicts. The square jowl,
that had grown deformed by the grinding of teeth
during the infliction of the lash, was an attribute
of each. Not one but had a terrible record —
according to the System’s notions; the least-
scourged of the lot had over 1000 lashes to his
debit.
“What will meet the case, Mr. Snowlee?”
asked Mr. Coxson.
“ By line 5, clause 15, section 9 of ‘ the Regula-
tions for Convict Discipline,’ chapter —,” began
the lord of Derndale.
“Oh, we’ll not trouble you about that, Mr.
Snowlee,” interrupted Coxson, with some im-
patience. “We know the maximum, but what’s
the least number that will properly punish these
fellows ? We want to be merciful, if we can.”
“ Certainly, your Worships,” said Mr. Snowlee.
“ And, under the circumstances, I think fifty
a-piece will do.”
And fifty a-piece it was, (And in the pocket-
book went down : “4 x 50 = 200.”)
Mr. Snowlee resumed his place on the bench,
and Mr. Smith got down, went into the witness-
box, prosecuted five of his men, and let ’em off
with five “dozen” between them. (Note-book
entry: “125.”)
Mr. Smith went up on the bench again—and
Mr. Jones filled the function of prosecutor for
three minutes, during which time he was successful
in inducing his magisterial brethren to distribute
amongst three men 150 cuts—one hundred to one,
a “ dozen ” a-piece to two. (Mr. Suchor noted:
“100 + 25) = 150.”)
And the defendants —the prisoners, what of
them? They had taken it all as a matter of
course. Two had plucked their forelocks as they
received their sentences, wi ih a cringing sub-
missiveness that would not h ve discredited a race
of hereditary serfs ; several had looked carelessly
about the Court-room while their offences were
under adjudication (if the proceedings of the
magistrates could be so termed) as though they
were the least interested parties present; but
none had demurred. As the jacketless rogue had
said : What was the use of demur or objection ?
The sooner the farce of the trial was over, the
sooner the real business of the day would be over
too, and the longer would be the interval between
their recovery and the next dose.
# * *
After Mr. Jones half-a-dozen other holders
of assigned labour submitted servants
to the tribunal. Six employers offered 25 servants
(including two women) before the shrine of
Immaculate Justice. Of the women, one was sent
to Parramatta Factory : “ She ’adn’t seen Mother
Gaden for a ’ol’ year, an’ w’u’d th’ beaks ’av th’
goodness to send her there ?” and the other was
ordered a week’s “cells,” As to the men, they
were awarded 700 among them. (The entry in
Mr. Suchor’s note-book stands ; “1 @ 100=100 +
(2+50) + (20+ 25)=700.”)
At half-past eleven o’clock (the court had opened
at ten) the magistrates adjourned for a quarter of
an hour —and a glass of wine.
While in the magistrates’ room a discussion
arose respecting the number of lashes.
“Counting the hundred to my man for con-
tempt of court, I make it only 1500,” said Coxson.
Snowlee was sure the total of stripes ordered
was 1600. In a pet, Coxson sent for the Clerk of
the Court and told him to bring the charge-book.
“I’ll wager you a pound, Snowlee, that I’m
right!”
“Wagering money is against the law and my
principles,” said Snowlee, “ but I’ll bet my coach-
man against your man, Jack the Shearer.”
Cunning Mr. Snowlee ! His Gazette memory
reminded him that his coachman had but three
months to serve to earn his ticket, and as the
fellow would not be trapped into committing an
offence, Mr. Snowlee would, in any contingency,
lose the man’s services at the end of the time.
Jack the Shearer, however, was a noted hand
with the shears and at all farm work, and Snowlee
coveted him, especially as four years would have
to pass before he could claim a ticket.
Particularly cunning Mr. Snowlee ! For had
he not added together the appointed lashes before
leaving the Court ? He was a man of principle,
but he had no objection to betting upon a cer-
tainty, and, of course, when Cox-son agreed to the
terms of the wager, a reference to the charge-book
showed that the former won the bet.
“ Well, I’ve lost,” said Coxson, closing the book
with a bang, “but, anyhow, sixteen hundred ain’t
so bad, is it ?”
* * #
When the Court re-opened at ten minutes
to noon, bluff Tom Coxson stationed himself once
more on the witness-stand. In the dock, holding
itself together with a courage that, if it had not
been manifested by a convict, would be styled
heroic; grasping the spear-points with swollen
and gory fingers ; in the distortion of its features
and in the compression of lips (a compression
which, while it stifled the cry of pain, sent the
sanguinary sweat oozing the more copiously
through the livid skin), losing its semblance of
humanity ; behind the bars, flogged and gashed,
stood a Creature —a Number —a Symbol of the
System.
It was the convict who had, as the first achieve-
ment of the morning, been awarded 225 lashes,
ftnd against whom his master was about to prefer
a charge of larceny. His name we know, and Ins
register-number; but of his earlier and later
history we know nothing. We have had no heart
to make the search. Had we enquired, we should
probably have found that his criminal history
began with the theft of a faggot valued at one-
shilling, or a bunch of turnips of the price of two-
pence ; and that his life ended on the gallows. A
score —two score —of such lives we have traced
from the starting point of their first committal in
the justice-room of a manorial mansion to the
final scene in some gaol quadrangle—where the
life went out as the warder’s bullet went in or on
the scaffold ; traced them through Assize Reports,
Hulks’ Registers, Convict - ships’ Manifestoes,
House of Correction Abstracts, Gaol Lists, Muster
Rolls and Schedules of Deaths ; traced them from
a happy rusticity to nameless fates, from innocence
to unutterable guilt; from Kentish hamlets or
Midland cities to that Norfolk Island graveyard
where the tear-dropping manchineel weeps silently
on three sides, and the sea moans on the fourth.
But this man’s “ record ” we have not traced. We
were afraid to investigate; for upon the margin ol
the charge-sheet, on whose entries we found con-
firmation of the essential statement of this narra
tive, there is inscribed this note :
This man always maintained that his tirst conviction in
England was wrongful. And from what has been reported
since he left the Bathurst district, there is reason to
believe his story was true.
We did not search out the man’s history, because
we feared to find it true. We did not desire to
add another case of wrongful conviction to our
list of innocent convicts. It was too long already.
# # *
Coxson was proceeding to state the facts of
the charge when Snowlee was roused to indignant
remonstrance.
“ Sergeant, how dare you allow the prisoner to
present himself before the Court so indecently
attired ?”
All eyes were fixed on the convict. When he had
got his flogging he had flung his shirt loosely over
his back and had tied it round his neck by the
arms. Over it, he had thrown his jacket. '
“Why isn’t the man properly dressed?”
demanded the Court.
“Well, your Worships,” replied the Sergeant
of Police, “ I didn’t think your Worships would
mind, as the prisoner has to be flogged again to-
day. If he had put his shirt on while his back is
as it is, the cotton would have knitted into the
cuts ”
“Well, what if it did?” snarled Coxson, who,
bear in mind, was not at this moment on the
Bench, but in the witness-box.
“ In that case, Mr. Coxson and your Worships,
the shirt would have brought the skin off with it!”
“ Your excuse can’t be allowed, Sergeant, ’ said
Mr. Snowlee. “The Regulations distinctly
stipulate that every prisoner of the Crown called
to appear before a Court of Petty or Superior
Jurisdiction must present himself in decent
attire.”
“ The man ought not to be before the Court,
your Worships; he is physically unfit,” continued
the Sergeant.
“ You are exceeding your duty, Sergeant,”
interrupted Mr. Snowlee. “The circumstance
that the man is before the Court is sufficient proof
of his capacity to attend the Court.”
“ That was the very observation I was about to
make, Mr. Snowlee,’ said Coxson. “Does the
prisoner produce a medical certificate that he is
unable to attend ? ”
Everybody smiled. Even the prisoner and the
Sergeant smiled ; even the muscles of the magis-
terial faces relaxed ! The idea of an assigned
man producing a medical certificate ! —how rich !
“Oh, never min’ —yer Washups,” gasped the
accused. “Let’s get alon’ with th’ biz-ness. I
dunno wot —I’m to be charged with—but I’m
guilty, I s’pose. We pris’ners are gen’ral’y guilty
w’en th’bosses charge us.”
And they “got along” with the charge. Mr.
Coxson accused him of stealing a loaf of bread
“ He’d kep’—me out o’ rashuns —fur two days,
yer Washups,” interjected the prisoner.
“Yes I did,” deposed Coxson, “but it was a
punishment. And he’d no right to overrule my
will and obtain food when I stopped his rations,
had he, gentlemen ? ”
Of course, he hadn’t. The prisoner was sen-
tenced to do three months in the Blackheath road-
gang, and do 25 lashes for “appearing in Court in
a grossly-indecent condition.” (Mr. Suchor wrote
down another “25.”)
“You’ll postpone the flogging your worships. ”
said the Sergeant.
“Let’s ’av it all —atwunst, Mr. Snowlee, sir,”
the culprit appealed. “’Not her dozen now won’
’urt much !’*
The Bench consenting to the prisoner’s request,
(“ Mr. Snowlee is a just and humane magistrate”
said Judge Burton in a foot-note to a printed copy
of a Charge) the Gov’rnent man was once more
shuffling off to the Flogger’s Corner when Mr.
Coxson stopped him. “Look here,” said his
master, “I’ll want you back when the three
months are up, you know—things will be getting
busy then what with the wool and harvest. Now,
don’t go and get into any more trouble in the
iron-gang and put in more time, d’ye hear ?”
“I tho’t I wasn’t wrong, sir,” the convict made
reply. “ I tho’t yer only put me ’way w’ile things
was a bit slack.”
One of the ingenious practices formed by the
System was the transfer to Government by in-
fluential employers of assigned labour of the cost
of maintenance of some of their hands during the
slack season. All that such a master had to do
was to obtain for the servant a sentence of three
months’ imprisonment for gang-labour. And very
few things were easier done than that, as the
reader will by this time understand.
* * #
After luncheon Mr. Snowlee appeared as
prosecutor for the second time, and bluff Tom
Coxson once more, figuratively speaking, assumed
the toga of the magistrate.
Thomas Dilby was charged by Mr. Snowlee with
being an absconder from Appin-road gang.
Thomas Dilby denied he was Thomas Dilby, said
that he was “ free, ” and that his name was William
Dickson ; had never been to Appin in his life, and
was on his way to Wellington Valley in search of
employment when he was arrested by Mr. Snowlee
on the road near Derndale homestead. Mr.
Snowlee deposed that he remembered seeing in the
Gazette of two years before a description of
Dilby, by which he at once identified the man
when he met him on the highway. Would their
Worships kindly search the index to the Gazette?
Their Worships, being in a kindly mood, did so,
and found a description of “Thomas Dilby, pef
Phoenix (3); native place, Essex; born, 1789;
height, sft. s|in.; complexion, fair ruddy; hair,
brown; eyes, hazel; small scar on right cheek,
impediment in speech ; tatooed with large figure
4 8 over D’ on breast.” When absconded Thomas
Dilby had in his possession a pass signed by Sir
John Jamieson, Kt,, J.P.
“ The prisoner, your Worships, ” further deposed
Mr. Snowlee, 44 is sft. 6in. high, but he may have
grown a half-inch in two years. No one, except
himself, can say, of course, when he was born, but
he looked as if he had been born in 1789; his
complexion is fair, his eyes hazel, his hair brown,
and he has a scar on his right cheek. He has not
now any impediment in his speech, but you know,
your Worships, that a prisoner sometimes assumes
a personal defect of that sort to mislead the
authorities, and he may have dropped the impedi-
ment when he made his escape. Neither had he,
when I caused him to be stopped, any pass signed
by Sir John. But, you know, gentlemen, the
first thing a convict would do would bo to destroy
his pass. Finally, your Worships, though the

BRUCE SMITH.
prisoner has not a huge figure of eight tatooed on
his breast, he has, as you will observe if you order
his chest to be exposed, an hour-glass tatooed
theieon over the letter D. And nothing, I respect-
fully submit, your Worships, can be easier than
to make the figure of eight into the design of an
hour-glass.”
By the Bench’s order, the man’s jacket and
shirt were torn open. There, sure enough, was
“au hour-glass over D ” tatooed with some skill.
Possibly, the outline might have been originally
an 8— but the odds were at least equal that the
drawing had never represented anything but the
toy of old Time.
Dickson, as the man called himself, Dilby, as
Mr. Snowlee contended he was, protested, and
protested in vain. The Bench decided he was
Dilby, and ordered his return to Appin gang,
there to be tried for escaping.
As he was being removed lie shook his fist in
impotent wrath at his accuser: “You per-
jurer ! You blackmailer ! I’ll murder you
if I get the chance !”
Mr. Snowlee was used to demonstrations of
that sort. He simply desired the Bench to pro-
tect him. And, keenly sensitive of the honour of
a brother magistrate, and of the necessity, in the
interests of the King’s Justice, of conserving the
bodily safety of a magistrate, they ordered this
Dilby, alias Dickson, to be flogged.
For threatening a Justice of the Peace they sen-
tenced him to receive 100 lashes.
For creating a disturbance in Court they sen-
tenced him to receive an additional 50 lashes.
For violating that regulation which required
every convict to answer truly as to his identity
when interrogated by a magistrate or officer of the
Penal Department, a further 50 lashes.
(Mr. Suchor here increased his total by “200.”)
How Dickson, otherwise Dilby, took bis flog-
ging ; how the others had taken theirs; how
nobly the twin scourgers, Messrs. Cracknecke and
Chucke.n, did their duty; how children looked
on and laughed as the greedy “cat” licked up
skin and blood ; and how the humble but indus-
tr ions ant held high carnival on the fragments of
human flesh that lay scattered in Floggers’
Corner, we shall tell you on another occasion.
* * #
As Mr. George Suchor was drawing down
William-street in his gig, he was hailed by a
relative.
“ Hello, George, didn’t know you were in town
to-day ?”
“Yes, Will; I heard Pugin (Pugin was the
Bathurst P.M.) was away on leave, so as I knew
Coxson and Snowlee were down on the roster, I
thought I’d just run in and see what they did.”
“ Well—how many?”
“ Only 1825, and sent a “ free ” man to a gang
on suspicion of being an absconder. Of course,
Snowlee was at the bottom there. Ten pounds
was the reward.”
“Ah! they’ve held, then, what Slyde, the
Sydney P.M., calls a ‘Field Day’—when he
gives more than 1500 lashes in one day, he calls it
that. Up to 1500, it’s only an ordinary parade.”
i “As well call it that as anything. But, good-bye
—I must be off.” He flicked his whip at his mare,
and, as she started, shouted : “ By the way, Will,
Snowlee told me to-day that the condition of
things couldn’t be better in this district than they
are. What do you think ?”
“ No more they could—from the Devil’s stand-
point.”
PRICE WARUNG.
The Australian Employers' Union, in one
of the reports which it cabled to dear old Hing-
land, states that “ public opinion is unanimously
favourable to the ship-owners, who have received
liberal subscriptions.” The whole tribe of Aus-
tralian liars, including the snake liar of the
country paper, and the Christian liar who lies on
behalf of the New Guinea missionary, and the
Jand-boom liar, and the liar who gets up the
building society’s satisfactory balance-sheet the
week before the great burst, and all the tribe of
loose, unassorted, unspecified liars who merely lie
in a general sort of way, are respectfully invited
to try if they can do or say anything to beat the
above.
Two hundred and fifty women employed
by a firm of London confectioners have just gone
on strike for the following reasons. They worked
from 12 to 14 hours daily, and were paid at an
average rate of 7s. a week, or a trifle over a penny
an hour. Any employe arriving late was fined at
the rate of 3d. per minute, which seems a trifle
exorbitant seeing that half an hour lost at this
ra‘e would extinguish a whole week’s wages in
one act. Any employe sitting down on any pre-
tence was fined 3d.—whether there was any addi-
tional charge for getting up is not stated. In
case of accidental breakage the offender was fined
three times the amount of the damage. For
singing or attempting to sing on the premises the
penalty was fid. ; for talking, Gd. ; for “ humming
a tune,” 2d. Through some unhappy oversight no
one seems to have assassinated the boss, although
for such an offence there was no extra charge.

Mv Dear Moorabinda, —
Behind my dimpled palm I would like to whisper you
that all the up-to-date society brides always have
trousseau and layette constructed in the one order -
probably on the same principle that makes a man take
his gingham along when he doesn't want it to rain.
Tlie first bride-elect to introduce this custom in Australia
need scarcely have exhibited so much shrewd foresight,
since she went to England to abide (N.B.— Lady Bertie),
but for those damsels who are settled down a hundred
miles beyond the civilised limit by their own Squatter
Augustus, the notion is a golden one. And it finds
favour inGussie’s eyes, too, since he isn't called upon, as
a rule, to foot the extended total of the embroidery-bill
Apropos to trousseaux, the day has passed—or almost—
when to marry meant to pile, clothing around yourself
as if your purchasing powers ceased with the days of
your celibacy. The modern bride now limits herself to
three or four frocks, and puts a gable, so to speak, on
her usual supply of under-linen, thus giving her husband
at least one hook in the doar whereon to hang his little
outbreak of finery. When the silly custom prevailed of
getting a dray-load of dresses and a mile of hats simply
because 3 r ou were about to halve your wardrobe or your
shoe-trunk with somebody e’sf>, the happy man had a
camping-out time of it. And as a man who has specu-
lated in silk socks and dress shirts to adorn the
marriage tie, and who knows how the last chapter of the
bill looked, yearns just as much for a deep, long, empty
drawer to put his trousseau away in as does his bride, it's
just about time the lady left him at least a hat-box all to
himself.
A corset-maker—a being who devoted her gigantic
intellect to getting 30 into 19-collapsed dismally in
Melbourne the other day, and her hopeless failure leads
one to wonder how it is that women who wouldn't wear a
ready-made boot to save their souls buy slop-stays
every time they want to brace up. It is nearly impossible
to get such things to fit; they are lumpy at the top, even
if slick enough at the middle, and who ever knew one that
really fitted round the hips ? Yet the average female goes
on blindly purchasing shapeless masses of whalebone,
whicH wear out much faster than corsets made to suit the
figure, quite content if they pinch her diaphragm horribly,
which means that they are “just her shape,” of course.
So long as she is squeezed to gasping-point, the local
“fashionable” does not ask for more, and, meanwhile,
the venturesome corsetifcre files her schedule, and beauty’s
misfit busks continue to be a prominent feature in her
skirtal outline.
The latest development of the itinerant “ society ”
actress—that of furling her skirts into Turkish trousers
when she sinks peacefully into a seat —makes your Sappho
reflect how very few people there are who manage their
skirts beyond criticism. Most individuals sit with the
lap of their frock sagging downwards inelegantly, others
have it drawn tightly across their knees while they
scrunch and sit on the rest of the drapery, a few haul
the skirt taut round one leg with a surplus of stuff
hunched up on the other side, and now on the stage we
have petticoats tightly wrapped round the two supports
as if to accentuate the fact that the wearer is a biped. Let
us hope the eccentricity will stay behind the footlights,
even though it is especially adapted to reveal the low-cut
shoe and open-work hosen.
N.S.VV. Women’s College fund now stands at £1586 13s.
3d. and as the canvassers are still running strong and well
there is every probability of their scheme coining off.
Miss Helen Phillips, of the Clergy Daughters School, has
been appointed tutoress to the girl-graduates from March,
1891, by which date the Women’s College supporters will
have to show “ Guv’ment ” the big cheque wisely insisted
upon or see the bubble hurst.
The indefatigable members of the committee have de-
cided—in response to an enquiry—that cookery and
domestic economy shall he included in the lofty curriculum
of theN.S.W. Women's College “if money is provided
for the same,” and if the college authorities consent. And
as even Dons know the universal importance of a grilled
chop it isn’t probable they will place many obstacles in
the way of the cookery lectures, oven though the subtle
aroma of boiled cabbage should pervade the great hall
itself. Cookery is a noble study, and I hope they won't
make it an “extra” at the ’Varsity; how much more
interesting the she-grad. will he when you know that she
combines science and buttered scones, and is not at a
loss when confronted by a doubly-dyed proposition or a
joint of raw pork.
Gov. Carington will hold a final “ morning dress” levee
at Government House on Ist November, at 11.30, when lie
will he supported by Lady C., who certainly deserves to
figure officially to the very last gasp. At a quarter after
three their then ex-vice-regalities leave Government
House for the last time for the railway-station, thence by
special train for Moss Vale, where they will spend Sunday,
going to Melbourne on Monday morning. Lord Carington
has privately expressed his intention of paying Sydney a
private visit at some future time, but it is positive that
local society has gaped its long last at his pretty, amiable
wife, after whom your Sappho metaphorically waves a
large, damp, farewell handkerchief.
More in sorrow than in anger yours truly has packed up
her best frock preparatory to flitting southwards per ex-
press in order to he present at the Melbourne Cup. There
will he a shocking dearth of the vice-regal lady at
Flcmington this year. Lady Carington may give the
gubernatorial smile, another stretch in front of the grand-
stand, but as she will only arrive in Melbourne on the
historic 4th, she may be tqo tired to attend. The dames
of the Cabbage Plot will thus be troubled with no
misgivings as to whsttiovingment 'Ouse will have on, nor
will they lie curdled in the midst of their riches by the
dread sight of the leading lady coming out in literal rags
as happened last year. Absence of Governor's wife means
peace of mind to the representative female who for 25
years running has appeared on the lawn each Cup Day
w ith £l5O on her back.
The opening of the big Rose-show happened in Sydney
Exhibition Building (the Society yearned in vain for the
jealously-guarded Centennial Hall), on 22nd inst., in the
presence of Lady Carington. The champion table (a
harmony in pale-pink roses and ferns) showed the same
old trick of more decoration than dinner - there hardly
being room for a knife and fork, and I do think it odd
that these decorations don’t give way awhile to the old,
tired “specimen” glass. An unnoticed table seemed
almost too original in its style. Placed at intervals were
gnarled roots of a fair size artistically filled with crimson
roses, ferns and grasses. It was a very fresh notion, but
as there was rather too much root to the square foot the
effect was cumbersome.
The crowd was rather an advance on former occasions.
Lady Carington, who looked very tired, wore copper-blue
silk designed in gold, sleeves and bodice-front of pale-blue
mull hound in gold straw, white lace and forget-me-nots ;
she also carried a presented bouquet, which looked like a
free fight in a spectroscope, there were so many colours
in it. Mrs. Burdekin (a judge) wore grey woollen with
pale-grey silk border, cuffs and skirt-panel of shag-brown-
strioed velvet; toque in shades of crimson roses and
leaves. Mrs. Haviland Reynolds (wife of the secretary)
were a charming Springish attire in cream delaine flowered
in heliotrope ; collar, sleeves and frontal scarf of heliotrope
silk, dainty bonnet of heliotrope, lisse and myrtle.
A stuffy-lookiiig novelty, which I vainly hoped would
he severely left to flutter fitfully from the “line” above
the draper’s counter, is the Raleigh ruff of cream lisse, to
he worn tightly round the neck of scraggy beauty. It is
a hot-looking addition, and transforms the most charming
creature into a sort of dog Toby on his hind paws ; it also
suggests the mumps. Nevertheless, a Sydney lady wore
one to the Rose-show, and the effect was strictly accord-
ing to schedule.
Lady Carington’s farewell reception at Government
House, Sydney, on 25th inst., was a crowded, hired-
carriage sort of ceremony, made notable by a large
attendance of men. “ Thousands of people ” were there,
and the Departing stood bravely on the verandah and
received the motley crowd, which arrived so thick and
fast that tlie ceaseless handshaking looked like as if Lady
C. was hauling a rope. Brother Rupert, got up to cocked-
hat-and-swotd-point, announced the guests to Her Ex.,
who wore a plain white silk dress, flounced round the
front and laced up the bodice, and trimmed with bands of
gold and silver galon. Mrs. Burdekin sported an artistic
gown in fawn-pink pongee designed in moss-green, the
sleeves and part of the bodice of guipure en suite, knots
of moss-green velvet here and there, and bonnet in black
jet, tulle and pink aigrettes ; Mrs. Vanderveer Green,
sage-green silk with panel and picture-sleeves of
dark-green Russell cord, toque of Russian violets ;
Mrs. Fairfax Ross, fawn and green checks with
bronze-green silk sleeves, toque of lilies of the valley ;
Miss Snowden Smith (the Primate’s chaperone) in black
silk and lace and jet bonnet trimmed with a Bacchanalian
t rimming of grapes ; the Misses Smith, in simple black
and without a word to say for tin mselves; Miss Dight
(who is to marry Captain Barlow), in pale-grey, tailor-
built toque of honeysuckle and black velvet; Mrs.
George Brock wore a unique frock in sunbeam-ye How
poplin slashed in guipure; Lady Innos, shot-pinkish
silk ; Mrs. B. R. Wise, white silk, white chip hat, and
feathers, knot of saffron roses on the bodice ; Miss Frances
Savile, pink pongee figured with green with pink sleeves
and sash, toque of pink and creamy feathers ; and so on
for three volumes. There was a band playing in the
garden, and tea, champagne, ices, &c.—a long, copious,
&c.—were served in the amber drawing and dining-rooms
whither the surges ever rolled to fortify themselves with
unlimited cake against the pain of parting. The Aus-
tralian-born Miss Myee Carington held her farewell levee
in the vestibule as the guests passed out, but even the
most representative gushers were not allowed to kiss the
interesting vice-regal addition, who concluded her recep-
tion with a dismal howl.
On dit that Lady Carington was observed to shed a
damp tear at the farewell reception. And no wonder.
If shaking hands with an army, each member of which
gripped you like a crab, didn’t bring the water to your
eyes you would be suspected of having an automatic arm.
Everything considered, it is a wonder the tears didn’t run
down the vice-regal cheeks all the time.
I am afraid I am hardly sentimental enough properly to
appreciate anybody’s “Romeo and Juliet.” I was not
thrilled when the Potter twined her snaky arms round the
Bellew, and even the balcony-scene could not make me
overlook the fact that Mrs. Potter had made her face up
like a third-row coryphee, and that her erotic smile looked
gummy on her re-appearance at Sydney Royal, on 25th
inst. But the gowns were there, as usual, and after all
the woman who knows how to dress is well worth watch-
ing. There are four frocks and a rohe-de-nuit in white
woollen, worn with white satin boots, which implies that
the hope of the Capnlets was in the habit of turning in
with them on, doesn’t it? It is in the minuet scene that
the prettiest gown occurs. It is of pale lavender-grey
poplin, the skirts edged with gold and silver galon,
the upper part of the long sleeves of silk, the
under part in long wing-like sweeps of grey gauze
spangled in silver ; the short bodice over the full
“chemise” waist of cream-silk was in green and gold
brocade with front of galon. Necklaces of pearls, a pearl-
girdle, and the usual latticed head-dress for ornaments ;
while the bouquet of actinotus (rather an anachronism, by
the way) was held in the same hand as a crushed straw-
berry scarf. As Juliet, Mrs. Potter generally grasps a
long piece of silk which has no visible connection with the
toilette, and one begins to think the passionate young
party has a perennial head-cold. In the Nurse scene,
Mrs. Potter wears primrose brocade, the skirt slit up
and laced over carubia satin ; the upper sleeves of oaruhia
satin, with the underpart of puffs of brocade, tied at inter-
vals with crimson laces, the front of the bodice in oaruhia
satin designed in gold, cap of oaruhia velvet with
two crimson roses, while a yard of yellow “Liberty”
is grasped in the hand as usual, fn the drug-scene
Juliet wears a ciel-hlue brocade covered with a sort of
all) in white muslin edged with silver through which the
blue shows mistily ; in the last scene ivory satin brocade
slit over an underskirt of silk and trimmed with gold
galon which was pretty enough to reconcile anybody to
any amount of bogus interment. As Romeo, Bellew
has captivating lesrs and generally sports a crimson silk
and damask satin get-up, which shows the fall in his hack
to the best advantage. Taking them “by and large” he
and his Juliet are a very well-tailored couple who pose
delightfully, but I wish they would not always exit hold-
ing their right arms as if they were hailing a cab.
U. ji- reported of a certain debonnair M.P. that on a
recent occasion lie kept a late train, half-fnfl of ladies,
waiting indefinitely. The “ orders ” were for the train to
hang about until he and his party came along, but the
vociferous objections of the passengers overcame red tape
and after much vigorous telephoning the procession
moved on after an hour’s delay. It afterwards transpired
that the courteous legislator never turned up at all at the
station. Such are Parliamentary manners.
Which reminds us that a gushing Toorakian person—
ever notoriously anxious to be in touch with the “ court
pawty ’’—once had the calmness to ask Lady Loch what
coloured gown she Intended to wear at the Cup? Even
that amiable mistress of diplomacy was unable to endure
the query and tried to snub the presumptous creature by
languidly replying: “ Really, Mrs. Dash, you had better
see my maid.” But the snub didn’t come off. Mrs.' Dash
did see the maid, or somebody, and the vice-regal toilette
w as one of twins on that occasion.
The man who possesses sixteen wifes’ sisters and a
culminating mother-in-law will be interested in the fact
that the English law-courts have decided that the husband
can forbid any lady whom he doesn’t fancy as an occasional
domestic addition, to cross his threshold, and should she
thereafter insist on calling, he can bring an action against
her and make the fair aggressor pay the piper 1
It is decidedly rough on the members of Sydney Phil-
harmonic Society that the “ Messiah ” is to he produced
on Xmas night. People who warble cannot do it effec-
tively on anything heavier than a mutton-chop and dry
toast, and thus the conscientious chorister will have to sit
cheerfully chewing his mutton while the turkey passes up
the other side. If he doesn’t, the choruses will sound as
if they were filtered through wadding. It is definitely
arranged that Madame Patey is to assist the society.
The departure of the little Caringtons (accompanied by
Miss Walenn) for England, per Orizaba, on 27th lust,
attracted a crowd of people-to the (juay. A number of
their little ex-playmates were there to wave farewell, and
Lord and Lady Carington, attended by Captain Helherl,
came to see them off. The vice-regal party have engaged
six cabins—Miss Walenn sharing her’s with Miss Cox, w ho
goes to England to marry Captain Trench. Lady Caring ton s
cabin is exactly 'amidships and has been especially fitted
in pink and primrose brocade, with a bouquet of waratahs
(a wiry decoration warranted to last out the voyage).
Lord Carington’s cabin is unadorned (except fora bouquet
of waratahs) at his special request. The Orizaba lias a
very full passenger-list.

The harbour picnic again rears its hydra-head. On 25th
inst. Mayor Burdtkin ran 130 Town Hall officials down
the harbour ; and on 18th inst. Mr. G. If. Reid gave a
most successful picnic on the sboresof “Our Great Natural
Advantage.” There were a compact number present,
including Mrs. Burdekin, Lady Innes, Hon. Rupert
Carington, and Captain Helherr, and the gay and festive
bachelor host was congratulated all round on the success
of his gipsy party.
A highly successful festivity was the ball given by Mr-
and Mrs. S. A. Fullford, “Norwood,” Hurstville, on 24th
inst. Mrs. Fullford received the guests in the vestibule,
and wore a trained gown of black silk with sleeves of
jetted ret, Elizabethan collar and front of Maltese lace
lace. A special train conveyed a large number of the
guests back to town at 1 a.in.

w hich made Miss Carrie Burton (daughter of Mr. Edwartl
Burton, editor of the Sydney Mail! Mrs. Frank Millington
on 23rd inst. The bridal gown was albescent, as usual, and
the three bridesmaids (Miss Mary and Miss Hetty Burton
and Miss Proctor) wore simple frocks of apricot mull and

principally—and as the bridal party left the church their

reception was held at Ounsler's, and at 8 o’clock the
happy pair left for Katoomba. Mr. Millington is editor
and part proprietor of the Gwydir Examiner, Moree.

Labour in Parliament.
Peak Bulletin, —The moment a social
movement becomes crystalised in a poli-
tical movement its doom is sealed. Creeds
are the shrouds of dead faiths, ceno-
taphs of evaporated inspirations. No sooner
does the democratic agitator enter the
councils of his country than he becomes
the mere catspaw of party, the formless
figure-head of a defunct cause. Everybody
has heard the anecdote of the would-be
traveller from Strasburg to Basle who, when
asking the price of his passage, was informed
by the captain of the boat that it was four
gulden on board and two if he helped to
draw the boat on the tow-path. The labour-
party in Australia is much in the same case.
Outside of the political rings its power is
formidable. Let it once allow its own per-
sonal representatives to enter the charmed
circle of unblushing intrigue and shameless
log-rolling and it immediately degenerates
into a convenient lever for the elevation to
power of a number of filibustering dema-
gogues and blatant caucus-manipulators,
whose sole recommendation to the suffrages
of the workmen are brazen effrontery and
the clever handling of that species of vulgar
buffoonery comprised by the tricks and
and shifts of cheap stump-oratory.
* # #
Labour in Australia has put its foot on the
verge of a new order. The present strike
has evidenced, and now evidences, a soli-
darity, a cohesiveness of interest little sus-
pected by the gymnasts of political life, or
the wire-pullers of party. I he present
strike may prove nothing more to Labour
than a moral victory rather than a substan-
tial success. Sobe .it! From the experience
of to-day the policy of to-morrow may be
drawn. If Labour is defeated now it will
learn a valuable lesson for future use. The
next strike, fought in a different way, will
be a battle to the death. The strikers will
have learnt that consideration and modera-
tion are thrown away in a conflict with an
insensate aggregation of money-bags. So
far, well and good ! But a danger threatens
the interest of Labour which bids fair to
nullify all the lessons which might have been
taught by the The alleged leaders of
the people show evident signs of turning a
purely social agitation, having for irs object
a reconstruction of the conditions which
regulate the distribution of wealth, into a
political trend for the'purpose of advancing
personal and party interests, and frigidising
a tremendous power into a mechanical force
for the regulation of the issues of the ballot-
box. No longer dictators, the Labour
Organisations will be regarded as so many
naughty children whom it is necessary to
placate with sugary promises, and hood-
wink into stupid acquiescence by drawing
side questions across the path of direct issues.
* * *
Labour is lost in Parliament! From
without politicians may be coerced, cajoled,
threatened and forced into representing the
interests of a compact party powerful
enough to drive them into obscurity. From
without Labour, can make the log-roller
sue for its suffrages. From within
Labour rapidly degenerates into a mere
tool for the political advancement of the
ambitious demagogues who may have tasted
the sweets of power and privilege for no
more than a single session. The Labour
vote, like the Orange vote, like the Local
Option vote, like the publicans’ vote, is
powerful enough to force the would-be
tribunes of the peojffe on their knees.
Labours own selected representatives from
Labour s own ranks are, however, invariably
human organisms who trade away their trust
for a permanent situation in proximity to the
Parliamentary refreshment-bar and the clubs
where votes are bartered over recherche
dinners. The chosen representative of
Labour may be an honest enough man when
he first crosses the threshhold of the Legis-
lative Chamber ; give him only time suffi-
cient, however, and he will awaken to his
true position. He is, first of all, a property-
less creature. He is not a specially able
man ; perhaps he lacks even the rudiments
of education. His air and his dress and his
speech, if he is a true blue workman and not
already possessed of undertaker’s livery, are
subjects for the ridicule of the House, butts
upon which the young “aristocratic” mem-
bers and the old Parliamentary hands dis-
charge the arrows of their practised persi-
flage. Smarting, irritated, in time humi-
liated by routine sarcasm, he seeks to make
his peace with the recognised authorities.
The “ situation ”is a comfortable one. He
drinks the choicest liquors, he eats the
choicest viands, he is treated with deference
and respect whenever his vote is wanted.
Gifted with a certain amount of shrewdness,
an ability to rave and bluster on fit occasions,
possessing a species of coarse wit which is
very valuable when he is badgered, and the
exercise of which often scores a point where
knowledge or eloquence would be thrown
away, he hastily discards the insignia of his
order, the outward and visible signs of
the cause he was chosen to advocate.
Ho adopts the silk-hat and the frock-coat
of respectability and vested stakes, he makes
himself acquainted with all the minutice of
parliamentary procedure, he conserves his
vote with adroitness, and speaks only upon
questions which appeal to the ballot-box.
In a brief period his corruption is complete.
He ceases to be a man, to be a workman and
the representative of workmen ; he becomes
an edger, a trimmer, a calculator, a turner
and twister, a cunning speculator whose
future as a politician is more to him than all
the interests of Humanity put together. He
dreads a return to the ranks of those he was
elected to represent and his life degenerates
into a frantic effort to hold on to the skirts
of whatever political faction or squalid
religious denomination he imagines to be
able to ensure his position as a holder of a
snug and comfortable situation.
# # #
The Labour party of Australia should
recognise the true condition of affairs. As a
party outside of political life it can compel
politicians to legislate for the cause of Labour,
It is in the magnificent position of a belli-
gerent dictating terms, and it has the power
to deal summarily with whomsoever betrays
its trust. Inside the House it can do noth-
ing ! It would in the hands of its own
representatives, chosen from ifs oWn ranks,
be degraded into an instrument for advertis-
ing to the country the names of men who
are ambitious to feather only their own nests
and to assure only their own ad»ancement.
A while since the most apparently advanced
of their tribunes in the New South Wales
Parliament a man whose name was synony-
mous with the core and inner heart of
socialistic democracy, could raise his voice
hardly high enough for the People who put
him into power. To-day he denounces as
“ fools and fanatics” whomsoever beneath
the sun should attempt the “ reconstruction”
of that Society whose need of reconstruction
gave him an opportunity of opening his
mouth upon the subject. The cause of
Labour is the cause of the People as a
whole, it is the cause of all, and that is a
narroAV view of the question which would
confine its interest to any organization or
any number of organizations which selected
the most cunning or the most successful
stump-orator to represent the interests of
Labour on a political board of directors con-
stituted apparently for the most successful
mode of dissipating the nation’s energy.
* * *
Politicians walk across the floor of the
House with every change of the political
Aveather-cock. Free-traders and Protec-
tionists, Customs-tariffers and Land-taxers
are apparently convertible terms—all sound
and fury, signifying nothing. With every
approaching election there is a change of
front. The boards of the Legislative As-
sembly Chamber are worn into runnels Avith
the tergiversation of those who have been
elected to tread them. Nothing is sure in
Parliamentary life but change; nothing is
certain but mutability. And is the Labour
Party foolish enough to imagine that the
Labour candidate would be truer to the
principles upon the power of which he
drifted into a legislative life than the ad-
venturer who forswears Protection because
he miscalculated the period of its advent?
No ! a thousand times rather would the
Labour candidate be the more susceptible,
inasmuch as he would the readier become
ashamed of the class and the principles he
represented, for no man is so anxious to hide
his opinions as the erstwhile workman, who
has learnt to wear a frock-coat, and to hide
also the callosities which he imagines to dis-
%ure his hands.
Amongst the representatives of wealth and
caste the workman has no place, unless he
be a very prodigy of mental force and iron
will, uniting all the strength and all the
virtues of all the men who have made history
for men, and such cast-iron heroes are
generally greater in themselves and possess
a greater power for good outside Parliament
than in it, John Burns, for instance,
while all-powerful outside, will probably
be a failure, will waste his time, in Par-
liament. Let the Labour Organisations
recall the experience of the past! Have
they one single man who was sent to Parlia-
meat upon their exclusive suffrages who now
remains true to their cause ? Notone! The
history of any single individual has been the
history of the whole number. To-day a few
smart adventurers in each of the colonies are
tempting the strikers to add another page to
the long history of their bitter experience,
and Ave ask them to remember and resist !
If they are true to this single principle they
will remain in a position which is practically
unassailable. If they are not, they will
simply repeat the silly practice of manufac-
timing puny copies of Bradlatjgh for the
Tory cause,
* *
The Sydney Morning Herald lately an-
nounced that it was, in its opinion, a very
desirable thing that the strike should be
settled before the elections came along,
otherwise the grand old cause of Freetrade
might be lost sight of and confused with a
Labour issue. The workmen now know
what to expect, they also know what course
to pursue. By all the means in their power
they must keep parliamentary candidates to
the close question of Labour issues, for if
legislation is not to deal with the only one
thing which demands legislation, i.e., the
prosperity of the commonwealth, we enquire
the use and reason of legislation at all. If
it is simply a description of trade protection
manipulated by a close ring of capitalists
and corner-operators, the sooner Labour
awakens to the fact the better, and every

man who has the cause of political purity at
heart should be extremely grateful to the
S.M. Herald for its timely information.
However, the working-men of Australia
have another lesson than this to learn from
the Herald’s utterance, and it is this :
“Keep out of politics yourselves, and com-
pel politicians to face you at the hustings on
a distinct Labour issue.” This, workmen of
Australia, is the only means by which you
can conserve all that you have already
gained, it is also the only means by which
you can gain anything in the future. The
House must not be allowed to diverge on
side issues ! Let the tariff alone for the
present, and give the denominational and
educational squabbles a severe rest! Select
your mouthpieces and keep them to the
point, but in the name of Progress hand not
over to your ambitious leaders —whose suc-
cess as organisers and administrators is no
guarantee of their fitness and stability as
parliamentary representatives of the demo-
cracy —a cause which is vital to your in-
terests, and which, moreover, they have
never served with fidelity in the past 1
# # *
Australian working-men are in political
matters usually no more suspicious than
children, perfectly prepared to believe
the promises of any demagogue who
possesses a specious flow of language and
a colossal amount of cheek. Of this
man, Labour Unionists, you must beware !
Do not permit him to use you as a stepping-
stone to a position in which you have always
hitherto been disgustingly “left.” Clothe
no trickster with the toga of your
authority. Use your power to the utmost;
keep your finger upon the political pulse as
persistently as you can, but enter not into
the political arena in the persons of your
own representatives. You have nothing to
expect from the man who is anxious to leave
your ranks, and everything to expect from
him who is compelled by the exigencies of
his public life to treat with you. Hold your
own without committing yourselves and you
will always be in the only position which is
practically impregnable—that of dictators.

Poor and Proud.
They are eight: the father, the wife, and six
children. The father, by occupation a draughts-
man, since the Exposition is without work. Credit
is exhausted. They owe the baker, the butcher,
the grocer; the rent is past due. The furniture is
pawned, sold ; nothing is left but a bed and some
mattresses too bad for the pawnshop.
The man has sought work at his profession, but
has found none. He is strong; he has good arms
and great needs ; he offers his strength. He will
work at anything, it matters not what; but every-
where he meets the same disheartening response :
“ No work for you !” Out of work, out of money,
and six hungry children. “God,” said Briollet,
“blesses large families—only, He does not feed
them,”
The neighbours see the Hyems go and come.
They salute those they meet with a sad good day,
and if they speak of their troubles it is to give
vent to a brief complaint—the endless complaint
of the resigned. “ Things go badly ! The times
are hard ! Life is full of sorrows !” (Vnd, closing
their door they keep their misery to themselves.
The burdens of fate accumulate, and one day,
tired of the barren struggle, they abandon it.
“ My children,” says the father, “ I am going to
die. I am worn out. When you are orphans,
perhaps you will be taken care of.” The wife then,
as she has since said, interposed : “You shall not
go alone. I will die with you.” Together they
had emigrated ; they had dragged their misery
from land to land, worked and wandered together,
bent under their burdens ever. And this last
journey he would take without her ! “No, dear
one, once more shall we move on together.”
Matters were decided with an heroic simplicity
“But the children?” Some were of an age too
tender to understand, like the last, who could but
say: “I’m hungry, mamma,” clinging to her
skirts. But some were grown, too. There were
Helene, Bertha, a pretty girl of 15, a brunette
with fine eyes. A newspaper recalls this morning
that paradox which Augustine Brohant dared
write in an album ; “ All sensible people prefer by
far dishonour to death.” Bertha totally lacked
sense ; she preferred death. She hesitated not a
moment. She had no thought that there are
means of evading starvation when one is a woman
with fine eyes. “That is it!” she cried, with
enthusiasm; “that’s it; let us all die together.”
“It will be as if we were asleep,” said Helene,
younger by two years.
Sunday, July 13, all the family went out. It
was a holiday; the streets were decorated; there
was dancing in the squares. This part of
Charbnne was in high gaiety. Paris was cele-
brating the fete of the Federation, which, a
hundred years ago, had thrown men into one
another’s arms. To the Republican legend that
ceremony had affixed its third sentiment,
“Fraternity”—which last Sunday was seen
written everywhere. The poor family wandered
among the joyous crowds, their sorrow made the
more sorrowful. Dejected, they went by hazard,
seeing what they would never see again, the
smaller lagging behind, as they turned to admire
longer these wonderful preparations—which made
them open wide their eyes—eyes that in a few
hours would be closeed for ever by a sinister death.
The father entered a wine merchant’s and
ordered two glasses of cognac, one after another.
“ I’ll pay for them to-morrow,” he said. Already
in the streets the tables were being arranged for
the kermesse, which was to last all night long and
even the next day. Perhaps some gallant, seated
at a table, excited with the day’s pleasures, might
have noticed with persistence that Bertha was a
fine lass. So much the more—this detail is given
us by the neighbours—since she had arranged her
hair with care for this last promenade, an un-
conscious act of coquetry which with most
women survives every catastrophe. But of the
looks fixed on her she felt neither the
caress, nor the invitation, nor the homage; she
passed along with her own, resigned and melan-
choly. And when, after returning home, the
father said : “Some one must go down and get
the charcoal!” she answered without hesitation :
“ I’ll go!”
They had saved 12 sous for the ghastly work.
She took them and sped away to the charcoal-
dealer’s. She wore a strange air, which struck
the people who noticed her. She remounted the
stairs. They prepared the charcoal brazier, one
helping the other. They stopped up the openings.
They embraced one another; they prayed together;
they lay down—the mother on the bed with the
youngest child ; the father near her; the others,
side by side, on the floor. And they waited, in
sleep or in tears. Toward four o’clock in the
morning, the father arose and relighted the
brazier. An hour of anguish followed. The
mother, who survived, has narrated, it seems,
that Helene cried out, that the father threw him-
self toward his daughter. But from that moment
the mother lost consciousness.
How long did the agony of each of these un-
happy ones last? They were found after four
days; their corpses were already decomposing.
Mine. Hyem, by a miracle, lived. When fresh
air was admitted to the room, she came out of her
lethargy, stretched herself out on the bed, and
saw around her the bodies of those whom she had
loved. Death had had the cruelty to let her live.
She spoke with coherence, her voice harsh, her
eyes in vacancy, frightened, frightful. And it
was only from her fragmentary utterances that
could be constructed, by supplying the omissions,
the history of the most fearful tragedy that desti-
tution has yet engendered.
“When a death by starvation is reported in the
streets of Paris, it is said, ‘ His own fault!’ ”
exclaims M. Jules Simon, in the Family Review
of the current fortnight. “ People hope to justify
themselves with that. Perhaps it was his own
fault; but certainly it is yours. We are all
responsible for the hopeless, for there would be
none if we did our duty.”
We have not done our duty by the Hyems. We
shall in vain, to put asleep our conscience, plead
an alibi, say we knew not that they were reduced
to such extremities, that they did not importune
the department of charities. For there remains
this mortal shame, this horrible crime; a father
and mother, brave, esteemed, honourable, indus-
trious, have killed their children and have willed
their own death, because worn out by hunger.
And that in high national fete, the day of the
centennial of the most beautiful of fetes, that of
fraternity. —Translated from Paris “ L’Eolaib.”
often seen in Ceylon papers, and is no mere joke.
It is by this means that the crocodile-hunters
secure their bait. It is no trouble for an English
crocodile-hunter to get these little children. The
Ceylon parents have full confidence in English-
men, and they will rent out their babies to be
used for crocodile-bait for a small sum. The Cey-
lon crocodiles are lazier than any other and are
harder to get. They lie for hours perfectly
motionless, basking in the sun. Hardly anything
can stir them. But when tempted by a fat Ceylon
baby placed on the banks of the stream, they
shake off their ennui, and their mouths water for
a delicate morsel of brown baby. The crocodile
gathers himself together and starts out for the
infant. When he gets about half-way up the bank
the hunter, concealed behind some reeds, opens
fire and gets his game. Then the baby is taken
home to its loving parents, to be used for the same
purpose next day.
The Red-Headed Girl.
Oh, give me the girl with the auburn hair!
The lass with the peachblow curls;
The one that the white horse always is neftie,
For she’s the boss of all girls.
The one whose topknot is flamingly red—
The colour we paint the town—
Of a rosy tinge, most luridly spread
O’er a carroty, sunset crown.
Oh, give me the girl whose cranium’s hued
With Harvard’s selected shade;
The tint of neckties worn by the dude,
The pink of the blushing maid.
The one with the wavy, raspberry bang,
That casts forth fiery rays;
Whose cherry bean-catchers witchingly hang
Like bunches of currants ablaze.
Oh, give me the girl whose hirsute tuft
Is of a rubicund hue;
Whose silken tresses, all frizzled and puffed,
Remind one of claret for two.
The girl whose crest like a radish glows
With a perpetual glare,
That scorches the soul till love hot grows,
And burns for the warm heart there.
Oh, give me girl with the ketchup crop,
Crab apple, cranberry tinged ;
Whose dome’s adorned with a tomato mop,
All crinkled and coiled and fringed,
With a sorrel Psyche knot on her pate,
Like grape juice, or old port wine—
With the coral, the ruby, garnet plait,
Like peppers or drug-store twine.
Oh, give me the girl with the gold-fish crimps;
With ringlets like beefsteak rare;
The one with the locks of friecaseed shrimps—
The one with the lobster hair;
The wienerwurst, frankfurt-sausage girl
With the soft shell crab-like braids,
For the one with the danger-signal curl
Lies over all other maids.
So give me the girl whose canopy’s down
Is rouge, like a toy balloon ;
Whose cupola’s capped with a scarlet crown
Of carnation, brick, maroon.
The one with the strawberry knob, so fair,
She’ll make things torrid through life ;
Oh, give me the girl with the raw meat hair—
The red-headed girl for a wife !
Frederick Goldthwaite, in N.Y, Clipper.

“The Conventional Lies
of our Civilisation.”
THE ECONOMIC LIE,-CHAPTER IV. (Concluded.)
BY MAX NORDAU.
1 cannot deny for a moment that the practical
realisation of this scheme would at first meet with
In uiy and difficult obstacles. The parents would
try to escape from the necessity of bequeathing
their property to the State by presenting it
to their children and others while they
were still alive. This would result in the
practical inheritance of a part of their patrimony
by the children, and it could only be prevented by
the State with difficulty. Butthis source of fraud
is of very small importance to the system as a
whole. The adoption of it would exert such an
influence upon the views and opinions of humanity
that they would soon be radically changed from
what they are at present. The parents would
learn to appreciate the fact that in the new, re-
organised community, lack of furniture did not
mean poverty and wretchedness for the child,
consequently the impulse to ensure a regular in-
come to it through life would become much
weaker. The State would find little or no trouble
in getting control of the notes, bonds, stocks, &c.,
w uch form the greater part of the floating capital
of the World ; all household goods, works of art
aid single objects of value might be exempted
f'O n confiscation and retained by the children as
m -mentoes of their parents ; there would be no
possibility for evading the law in the matter of
real estate. But the most important, indeed, the
only essential point of the whole system is this :
the land with all the hous-s, builiings, factories,
trading establishments, &c., that are on it, must
become the unalienable property of the community
and come into its direct possession at the close of
each generation. Anyone desirous of owning land
or factories, will receive a title to them for his
life-time from the State, for which he must pay
an annual rental, which will be a certain per-
centage of the total amount of capital repre-
sented.
* # *
This idea is no unprecedented revolutionary in-
novation, as some would suppose, but merely the
further development of certain conditions existing
at the present day in many countries, especially
in England and Italy. In these countries there
are many landed proprietors who do not culti-
vate their land with their own hands, but rent it
as tenant-farmers. There is nothing to prevent
society from placing the manufacturer and the
tillers of the soil upon the same footing as the
English tenant farmer, with one great proprietor
as the master of them all : the State. This
arrangement of the economic world would make
it possible for the single individual to accumulate
personal property by his sagacity and industry as
at present, although not to such an enormous
extent as the fortune; of the pirates and parasites
of our modern civilisation. The talented, the
industrious man would find in a more luxurious
manner of living the reward for Ins greater ability
or efforts, mediocre capabilities and the indo-
lent man would be obliged to live more frugally,
while the individuals who shirked or refused to
work would be the only ones condemned to want.
The accumulation of enormous quantities of land
in the possession of one single tenant could not
occur, as he would experience such difficulty in
finding labourers to till his land, owing to the fact
that as anyone willing to work could rent land
from the State, no one would have any induce-
ment to drudge for another when he could lie his
own master and enjoy the blessings and fruits of
independence. The development of the system
leads necessarily to a condition in which each
individual would require only so much land as he
alone, or with the help of his family, could suc-
cessfully cultivate. The unnatural development
of manufacturing industries at the expense of the
agricultural would thus be prevented. For as
the individual would have it in his power to
become an independent farmer as easily as a
factory operative, he would not enter upon the
latter career unless it offered him a pleasanter
and more profitable existence than farming,
and the multitudes now seeking work in such
numbers in the factories, underbidding each other,
and satisfied with the very smallest p ssible
amount of life’s goods and enjoyments, such a
class would be inconceivable in a society re-
organised according to this system. Real
difficulties in carrying it out would not arise
until the country became too densely populate 1
and the soil exhausted. When these conditions
arrive, and it is found impossible to supply all
demands for productive land and factories, then a
part of the young people must decide upon
emigration. However, an extremely intensive
cultivation of the soil, such .as I mentioned above,
will postpone this necessity to a far-distant future.
9 9 9
There is no doubt but what this system is a
kind of Communism. But let him who shudders
and turns pale at this word remember that we are
living now in the midst of a complete Com-
munism, only it is a passive instead of an active
Communism. We have no community in posses-
sions, but we have a community in debts. No
one is shocked at the fact that every citizen,
merely on account of his being a citizen of the
State, is debtor to an amount varying in different
countries —in some countries it is over £lOO
per capita. Why should anyone be shocked
at the idea of the citizen owning, instead of
owing, in consequence of a complete revolution, a
corresponding amount of property, if the State
should possess common property as well as
common debts ? In such a case the State would
not be always taking taxes from its citizens, but
distributing benefits among them, as it now does
to a small number of them, comprising the privi-
leged class. Besides, the State already possesses
property of all kinds in buildings, lands, forests,
ships, &c. The existence of this property, which
is certainly not individual possessions, but
belongs collectively and individually to all the
citizens together, is certainly communism, but
it is not recognised as such by the people because
the forms of government and the public institu-
tions inherited from the Middle Ages favour the
idea that this, common property is an individual
property belonging to the king or ruler of the
state whoever he may be. The public debts,
public property, or taxation are not the only forms
in which Communism exists in our civilization.
Certain kinds of credits are nothing hut the
rankest Communism. When one man lends
another money from his pocket, or offers him a
draft secured by a mortgage, upon his private for-
tune, which is accepted by a third person like so
much cash, then it is practically an exchange of
individual property. But when a bank offers un-
secured notes in circulation—and in many banks
Uie amount of unsecured notes is a third or more
of the entire number of notes in circulation—and
gives a man in exchange for his signature on a
note a number of these Unsecured notes with
which he can go forth and buy anything he wishes,
then the transaction is an act of the most com-
plete Communism. The bank does not give its
saved-up labour, that is gold, but a certificate for
certain labour to be performed in the future. The
fact that the community will give up goods, re-
ceiving these unsecured notes in exchange, is a
proof of the respect in which mankind holds this
principle of human solidarity and a recognition of
the concomitant fact that the individual member
of the community has a right to share in the goods
exist ng in it, even when he cannot offer in ex-
change for this share any personally-produced
equivalent.
# # *
The absorption of all goods into the public pro-
perty after the death of the accumulator would
lead to an almost inexhaustible public fund,
without interfering with individual possession.
Each member of the community would have
then his individual and general property as he
has his baptismal and family name. The pub-
lic property with which he is born is like
his family name, the private fortune which
he accumulates during the course of his
life and of which he is the sole, unmolested pro-
prietor and usufructuary, is his baptismal name,
and both taken together represent his economic
personality as the names represent his personality
as a citizen. While he is toiling for himself he is
working for the community, which will some day
fall heir to all the surplus remaining after his ex-
penditures have ceased. The public fortune will
be a vast reservoir, receiving the surplus of the
rich and dealing out blessings to the poor, regain-
ing its normal level once in every generation and
thus equalising the inequalities in the distribution
of property, which inheritance on the contrary,
fixes indelibly and increases in each generation.
# * *
To such a new arrangement of the pdtieo-
economical organisation of society the world must
come at last, because reason and the ideas of man-
kind in regard to man and the universe ba ed
upon natural science demand it One single
fundamental principle must govern society, and
this principle must be either individualism, that
is, egotism or the solidarity, the cohesive fellow
ship of mankind, that is, altruism. At the present
day neither fellowship nor egotism are ruling
alone, but a combination of both, which is as un-
reasonable as it is inconsistent. Possession is
organised upon a personal basis and egotism
reaches in the laws governing inheritance the
utmost limits to which it can attain, by not only
seizing by stealth and violence everything that it
Can lay bands on, but by clinging to the plunder
for ever and ever and excluding the rest < f man-
kind from ever sharing in its benefits. The man
of property, however, will not allow the Ulan with-
out property to call that princi le to his aid to
which the former owes his wealth. Fortum s
are accumulated in the name of, iudi idualism ;
but they are defended in the name of human
solidarity. The rich man enjoys his dis-
proportionate share of life’s blessings of which he
has made himself master by unblushing egotism ;
but when the poor man helps hi nisei to them with
some of the rich man’s egotism and selfishness, he
is arres'ed. In the form of usury and speculation
the un crupulous furtherance of self-interest is
allowable, but it is strictly forbi deli when it
takes the form of robbery and theft. The same
principle applied in the former case is a meiit, hi
the other a crime. Human reason r. volts at s eh
ideas. If egoti misto be preached, let it be con-
sistent and assert its right in all cases. If it is
right for the rich man to luxuriate in a life of
leisure because he has been able to get po session
of landed estates or to take advantage of the
labour of others, then it must also be conceded to
by right for the poor man to strike him dead and
take possession of his property as the spoils of
victory, if he has the courage and strength to
undertake and carry through such an undertak-
ing. This is logical. It is true that such logic
would soon bring society to destruction and our
civilisation to the dogs, and men would become
like beasts of prey wandering alone through the
land and tearing each other to pieces, But anyone
who is not pleased with this abstract aim of our
social development, egotism, has ro other alterna-
tive before hun but to accept the other sole prin-
ciple, fellowship. The motto will no longer be :
E' eryone for himself; but: One for all and all for
each. Society will then assume the responsibility
of supporting and educating the youth of the
country until they can earn their own livelihood,
of supporting those too old and feeble to support
themselves, of coming to the aid of infirmity,
without allowing hunger and distress to exist
except as the punishm ent of voluntary idleness.
But these responsibilities can only be accepted
and fulfilled upon one condition : the abolition of
the right of inheritance.
9 9 9
Great catastrophes are looming up on the field
of political economy and it will not be possible to
ignore them much longer. So long as the masses
were religious, they could be consoled for their
wretchedness on earth by promises of unlimited
bliss in the future. But to-day they are becoming
more enlightened, and the number of those pat ent
sufferers is daily growing less who find in the
Host a satisfactory substitute for their dinner and
accept the priests' order on the place waiting for
them in paradise with as much pleasure as if it
were some good terrestrial farm of which they
could take immediate possession. The poor count
their numbers and those of the rich and realise
that they are constantly growing more numerom
and stronger than the latter. They examine the
sources of wealth and they find that speculating,
plundering and inheriting have no more rational
justification for existing than robbery and theft,
and yet the latter are prosecuted by the laws.
The increasing disinheritance of the masses by
their deprivation of land and by the increasing
accumulations of property in the hands of a few,
will make the economic wrongs more and more
intolerable. The moment that the millions acquire
in addition to their hunger, a knowledge of the
remote causes to which it is due, they will remove
and overthrow all obstacles that stand between
them and the right of sati fying their appetite.
Hunger is one of the few elementary forces which
neither threats nor persuasion can permanently
control. Hence it is the power which will pro-
bably raze the present structure of society level
with the ground, in spite of its foundations of
superstition and selfishness —a task beyond the
power of philosophy alone.

The Needles Patent Gas-making Machine;
The Harvey Patent Safety Kerosene Lamp;
And the New Era Portable Gas Lamp.
The Harvey Lamp has been adopted by the
Railway Commissioners for lighting the Sydney
trams. We were awarded the Highest Order of
Merit and Special Mention by experts at. the
late Melbourne Exhibition for our lighting
mediums. Over 6000 Harvey Lamps are in use
in this colony, and for all lighting purposes are
giving universal satisfaction. These Lamps can
be seen on one of the North Shore ferry-boats,
and are suitable for all classes of vessels. The
Inventor offers £2OO to anyone producing as good 1
